Taqlid in Tawhid and the Priority of Knowledge
Poetic discussion on taqlid in tawhid and the obligation of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the poet's position on taqlid in tawhid, including the inherent hesitation, the condition for its sufficiency, and the affirmation of knowledge as the first obligation.

Core Idea
Faith based on imitation contains hesitation; certain knowledge is the first duty.

In this passage, the author addresses the critical issue of taqlid (imitation) in the fundamental beliefs of tawhid. He points out that such faith is not free from hesitation, then highlights a scholarly disagreement on whether certainty obtained from another's statement suffices. Finally, he asserts that the foremost obligation is knowledge, noting a difference of opinion on this matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then read the translation. Notice the flow from the general statement about taqlid to the disagreement and then to the final decree about knowledge.
The passage is poetic and concise; each line carries significant meaning.
2
Identify the hesitation in taqlid
The first line states that faith based on taqlid does not lack hesitation. Reflect on what hesitation means here – a lack of full certainty. This sets the stage for the discussion.
3
Understand the disagreement and condition
The second and third lines present a difference of opinion: some scholars report a disagreement, others have verified kashf (unveiling). The key condition is stated: if one achieves certainty (jasm) through another's statement, it suffices; otherwise, he remains in harm. Note the emphasis on jasm.
Jasm means firm, unwavering certainty.
4
Grasp the final point on knowledge
The last line commands to be certain that the first obligation is knowledge (ma'rifa), and notes there is a disagreement attributed on this. Consider why knowledge is prioritized over taqlid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ فِي التَّوْحِيدِ إِيمَانُهُ لَمْ يَخْلُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدٍ فَفِيهِ بَعْضُ الْقَوْمِ يَحْكِي الْخُلْفَا وَبَعْضُهُمْ حَقَّقَ فِيهِ الْكَشْفَا فَقَالَ إِنْ يَجْزِمْ بِقَوْلِ الْغَيْرِ كَفَى وَإِلَّا لَمْ يَزَلْ فِي الضَّيْرِ وَاجْزِمْ بِأَنَّ أَوَّلًا مِمَّا يَجِبْ مَعْرِفَةٌ وَفِيهِ خُلْفٌ مُنْتَسِب
Since everyone who practises taqlid (imitation) in tawhid, his faith does not lack hesitation. Concerning this, some of the people report a disagreement, and some of them have verified the uncovering (kashf) in it. So he said: 'If he is certain by the statement of another, it is sufficient, otherwise he continues in harm.' And be certain that the first of what is obligatory is knowledge, and in it there is an attributed disagreement.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage discusses the issue of taqlid (blind imitation) in matters of tawhid. The author states that faith based on taqlid is not free from hesitation. Then he mentions a difference of opinion: some scholars hold that if one achieves certainty (jasm) through the statement of another, it is acceptable; otherwise, it is harmful. Finally, he affirms that the first obligation is knowledge, but there is a disagreement attributed on this point.

Key Terms
تقليد
ترديد
جزم
كشف
معرفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think all taqlid is invalid and unacceptable.
Correction: The poet allows taqlid if it produces certainty (jasm); otherwise it is harmful. Taqlid is not inherently invalid but must lead to firm belief.

Checkpoint
What is the poet's view on faith based on taqlid?
A) It is perfect and sufficient.
B) It does not lack hesitation.
C) It is entirely invalid.
D) It is always harmful.

Key Takeaway
While taqlid in tawhid implies hesitation, it can be valid if it results in certainty; nonetheless, the first obligation is knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirmed your faith in Allah, was it through imitation of your family or society, or did you seek certain knowledge? Can you pinpoint any area where your faith still carries hesitation? What will you do today to replace imitation with certainty?
?
If the first obligation upon you is knowledge, how much time have you devoted this week to studying the essentials of tawhid? Are you satisfied with your level of understanding, or have you been content with inherited beliefs? Commit now to a small daily action to increase your knowledge of Allah's attributes.
?
The poet warns that without certainty from taqlid, you remain in harm. Reflect on the 'harm' in your own life—what doubts or spiritual weaknesses trouble you? Are you willing to ask a scholar or seek a reliable source to turn that hesitation into certainty? Write down one step you will take before the week ends.

Taqlid in Tawhid and the Requirement of Certainty
The poet discusses differing opinions on whether taqlid in tawhid is sufficient or requires certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether taqlid (blind imitation) in tawhid is sufficient for faith, and the condition of certainty in accepting transmitted belief.

Core Idea
Taqlid may suffice if it leads to certainty, but it inherently lacks full assurance.

The poet addresses a critical question in Islamic theology: can one simply imitate the beliefs of others in matters of tawhid, or is personal certainty required? This passage presents two scholarly positions on the validity of taqlid in tawhid, highlighting the importance of certainty for sound faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the poet's statements about taqlid and the ensuing debate.
Notice the contrast between hesitation and certainty.
2
Identify the two scholarly positions
The poet mentions two groups: one that reports a dispute (khilaf) and another that has verified an unveiling (kashf). Focus on the quoted opinion about certainty from another's statement.
3
Reflect on the condition of certainty
Consider why certainty (jazzm) is emphasized. The poet indicates that without certainty, one remains in harm. How does this relate to the validity of faith?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ كل من قلد في التوحيد إيمانه لم يخل من ترديد ففيه بعض القوم يحكي الخلفا وبعضهم حقق فيه الكشفا فقال إن يجزم بقول الغير كفى وإلا لم يزل في الضير واجزم بأن أولا مما يجب معرفة وفيه خلف منتصب
For everyone who practices taqlid (blind imitation) in tawhid, his faith does not lack hesitation. Concerning this, some people relate a dispute (khilaf) and some of them have verified the unveiling (kashf). So he said: 'If he is certain (yajzim) by the statement of another, it suffices; otherwise, he remains in harm.' And be certain that the first thing that is obligatory to know is [this], and concerning it there is an established disagreement.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage addresses the controversial topic of taqlid in tawhid. The poet asserts that whoever follows blindly (qallada) in matters of tawhid, his faith is not free from doubt (tardid). He then mentions that some scholars report a dispute (khilaf) on this issue, while others have investigated it and reached a clear conclusion (kashf). One opinion holds that if the person attains certainty (jazzm) by accepting the statement of another, then it suffices; otherwise, he suffers loss. The poet then commands the reader to be certain that the first obligation is knowledge, but there is disagreement on this.

Key Terms
قلد
توحيد
إيمان
ترديد
خلف
كشف
جزم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think taqlid is always invalid in tawhid.
Correction: The passage shows a scholarly view that if taqlid leads to certainty, it may be acceptable.

Checkpoint
What is said about the faith of one who practices taqlid in tawhid?
A) It is perfect
B) It is free from doubt
C) It is not free from hesitation
D) It is invalid

Key Takeaway
Taqlid in tawhid is a disputed issue; some scholars accept it if it yields certainty, but the poet notes that it inherently involves hesitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider your own belief in Tawhid. Is there any aspect you hold simply because you were taught it, without personal certainty? The poem warns that such imitation leaves hesitation. Where do you feel doubt, and what concrete step will you take this week to transform that area into certainty?
?
The poem presents two scholarly responses to taqlid. Which response do you find yourself leaning towards? Are you comfortable with merely accepting the view of a scholar without understanding, or do you seek personal conviction? How does your answer affect your responsibility to actively seek knowledge of Allah?
?
The poet stresses that the first obligation is knowledge. Have you prioritized gaining knowledge of Allah over other religious activities? If not, what is one change you can make in your daily routine to pursue that essential knowledge, even if it means sacrificing comfort or time?

Iman as Tasdiq and the Dispute on Utterance
The poet defines iman as tasdiq and presents two views on utterance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that iman is defined as tasdiq (inner affirmation) and that scholars differed on whether utterance (nutaq) is a condition or a part of iman.

Core Idea
Iman is heart affirmation; utterance is contested as condition or part.

The poet begins by establishing the core definition of iman as tasdiq, settling the foundation. He then introduces a key dispute: is verbal utterance a mere condition for the validity of iman, or is it an integral part of it? This passage sets the stage for understanding the relationship between inner belief and outward expression.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text aloud or silently. Notice the key terms: إيمان, تصديق, نطق.
The poet's style is concise; each term carries weight.
2
Identify the definition
The passage states 'وفسر الإيمان بالتصديق' — iman is interpreted as tasdiq. What does tasdiq mean? It means confirmation or affirmation of the heart.
3
Examine the dispute on utterance
The poet mentions two opinions: one says utterance is a condition (shart) like action, another says it is a part (shatr). Understand the difference between condition and part.
A condition is required for validity but external; a part is an intrinsic component.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider how these views affect the definition of iman. If utterance is a part, then iman includes speech; if a condition, iman remains essentially inner affirmation.
This dispute has practical consequences for judging who is a believer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفسر الإيمان بالتصديق والنطق فيه الخلف بالتحقيق فقيل شرط كالعمل وقيل بل شطر والإسلام اشرحن بالعمل مثال هذا الحج والصلاة كذا الصيام فادر والزكاة ورجحت زيادة الإيمان بما تزيد طاعة الإنسان ونقصه بنقصها وقيل لا وقيل لا خلف كذا قد نقلا
And iman is interpreted as tasdiq (affirmation), and concerning pronunciation (nutaq) there is a dispute (khilaf) upon verification. Some said it is a condition (shart) like action, and some said it is rather a part (shatr). And Islam, explain it by action. An example of this is Hajj, prayer, likewise fasting, understand, and Zakat. And the increase of iman is strengthened by what increases the obedience of a person, and its decrease by its decrease. And some said no, and some said no (i.e., denial), and a report like this has been transmitted.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of iman (faith) and its relationship to Islam. According to the poet, iman is defined as tasdiq (inner affirmation). There is then a dispute about 'nutaq' (utterance) — whether it is a condition (shart) for the validity of iman, similar to action (amal), or whether it is actually a part (shatr) of iman itself. The poet then tells us to explain Islam as action (amal), giving examples: Hajj, prayer, fasting, and Zakat. He then addresses the topic of increase and decrease of iman: the preferred view (rujjiha) is that iman increases with increased obedience and decreases with decreased obedience. However, some deny this (qila la), and this difference of opinion has been transmitted. The poet uses repetition 'qila la wa qila la' emphasizing the denied stance.

Key Terms
إيمان
تصديق
نطق
شرط
شطر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think iman is simply verbal confession without heart affirmation.
Correction: The poet states iman is tasdiq (heart affirmation) first, and utterance is a separate matter.

Checkpoint
According to the poet, what is the definition of iman?
A) النطق باللسان
B) التصديق بالقلب
C) العمل بالجوارح
D) جميع ما ذكر

Key Takeaway
Iman is fundamentally heart affirmation; the status of verbal utterance is a matter of scholarly dispute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars agree that iman is fundamentally tasdiq—heart affirmation. When you say 'I believe,' does your heart truly affirm what your tongue declares? What is one area of faith where your inner conviction feels weak, and what concrete step can you take to strengthen it today?
?
The dispute on utterance reminds us that iman is not merely words. Have you ever reduced your faith to outward expressions while neglecting the heart? Before you sleep tonight, examine your inner state: is your tasdiq alive or dormant? Commit to a specific dhikr or reflection to revive it.
?
If iman is primarily inner affirmation, then no one can truly know another's iman except Allah. How does this affect the way you judge others who fall short in outward practice? Will you commit today to leave judgment to Allah and focus on nurturing your own tasdiq?

Islam as Action and the Increase/Decrease of Iman
The poet defines Islam as action and discusses increase/decrease of iman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Islam is defined by actions such as prayer and fasting, and that iman increases with obedience and decreases with disobedience, though some deny this.

Core Idea
Islam is action; iman waxes and wanes with obedience.

The poet continues by explaining Islam as action (amal) and gives concrete examples: Hajj, prayer, fasting, and Zakat. He then addresses the debated topic of whether iman can increase or decrease. The preferred view affirms increase and decrease based on obedience, but a contrary view is also reported.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read from 'والإسلام اشرحن بالعمل' to the end. Notice the examples and the contrasting views.
The poet uses 'اشرحن' meaning 'explain' in an imperative form.
2
Understand Islam as action
The poet tells us to explain Islam as action. The examples given are Hajj, prayer, fasting, and Zakat – the pillars of Islam.
This connects Islam to outward deeds.
3
Analyze the increase and decrease of iman
The poet presents the strengthened view (رجحت) that iman increases with increased obedience and decreases with decreased obedience. Then he mentions that some deny this (قيل لا).
This shows there was a historical debate.
4
Reflect on the second 'قيل لا'
The poet repeats 'قيل لا' and says a report like this has been transmitted, emphasizing that the denial is also a known position.
The repetition may stress the authenticity of the opposing view.

Source Text

And some said no, and some said no (i.e., denial), and a report like this has been transmitted.
Source pages p. 82

However, some deny this (qila la), and this difference of opinion has been transmitted. The poet uses repetition 'qila la wa qila la' emphasizing the denied stance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own obedience to Allah, can you identify a specific action that has noticeably increased your iman? What will you do today to repeat that action?
?
The passage defines Islam as action. In which area of your life is your faith not matched by your actions? What is the first step you will take to align them?
?
If iman truly decreases with disobedience, think of one sin you keep returning to. How has it affected your connection with Allah? Are you willing to leave it to increase your iman?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner

Faith Defined: Tasdiq, Utterance, and Works
Defines iman as tasdiq, explains dispute on nutq, distinguishes islam, and discusses increase/decrease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of faith as inner affirmation, the scholarly dispute over the role of utterance, and the relationship between faith and works, including the view that faith increases and decreases.

Core Idea
Faith is inner affirmation; works are Islam, not part of the definition but connected.

This passage from Jawharat al-Tawhid lays the foundation for one of the most debated topics in Islamic theology: the definition of faith (iman). The poet clarifies that faith is essentially tasdiq (affirmation of the heart), but immediately notes a difference of opinion regarding the verbal utterance. He then distinguishes faith from Islam, which is expressed through actions, and presents the preferred view that faith increases with obedience and decreases with disobedience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the key terms: tasdiq, nutq, amal.
The passage is concise; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the definition of faith
The poet says 'wuffira al-iman bi al-tasdiq' – faith is explained as tasdiq. This is the core definition. Understand what tasdiq means: inner conviction.
The Ash'ari position is that faith is tasdiq alone.
3
Examine the dispute over utterance
The poet mentions two views: some say utterance is a condition (shart) like action, others say it is a part (shitr). Reflect on the implication: if utterance is a condition, faith can exist without it; if a part, then utterance is essential.
This dispute affects whether a mute person can have faith.
4
Understand the distinction between iman and islam
The poet says 'al-Islam asharhu bi al-amal' – Islam is explained by action. So actions belong to Islam, not necessarily to the essence of faith. Then examples: Hajj, prayer, fasting, zakat.
This aligns with the hadith of Jibril distinguishing iman, islam, and ihsan.
5
Analyze the increase and decrease of faith
The poet presents the preferred view (rajjaha) that faith increases with obedience and decreases with disobedience. He also notes that some deny increase/decrease, and that there is transmitted a report of no disagreement.
This is a major point of controversy between Ash'aris (increase but not decrease) and Salafis (both increase and decrease).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفسر الإيمان بالتصديق والنطق فيه الخلف بالتحقيق. فقيل شرط كالعمل وقيل بل شطر والإسلام اشرحن بالعمل. مثال هذا الحج والصلاة كذا الصيام فادر والزكاة. ورجحت زيادة الإيمان بما تزيد طاعة الإنسان ونقصه بنقصها وقيل لا وقيل لا خلف كذا قد نقلا.
Faith is explained as tasdiq (affirmation), and the utterance (al-nutq) in it is a point of dispute in verification. It was said: [it is] a condition like action, and it was said: rather a part, and Islam—I will explain it—is by action. An example of this is Hajj, prayer, fasting, and zakat—so understand. And the increase of faith by what increases the obedience of man is preferred, and its decrease by its decrease; and it was said no, and it was said no disagreement; thus it has been transmitted.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage addresses the definition of faith (iman). The poet states that faith is tafsir bi al-tasdiq (explained as inner affirmation), while the utterance (nutq) is disputed: some consider it a condition, others a part. Then he clarifies that Islam (outward submission) is by actions like Hajj, prayer, etc. The majority view (rajjaha) is that faith increases with obedience and decreases with disobedience, though some deny increase/decrease, and others claim no real disagreement.

Key Terms
الإيمان
التصديق
النطق
العمل
الزيادة والنقص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that actions are an essential part of faith (iman), such that faith cannot exist without them.
Correction: The poet distinguishes iman (tasdiq) from Islam (actions). Utterance and actions are conditions or parts of Islam, not necessarily of the essence of faith.

Checkpoint
According to the poet, what is the preferred view on faith and works?
A) Works are not part of faith
B) Faith increases and decreases with works
C) Faith is only tasdiq
D) Works have no effect

Key Takeaway
Faith is inner affirmation, while actions belong to the domain of Islam; faith can increase and decrease according to one's obedience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If faith increases with obedience, what does the state of your obedience today tell you about the state of your iman? Are you content with it, or does it stir you to action? What specific act of obedience will you commit to today to increase your faith?
?
The passage shows a disagreement on whether utterance is part of faith. When you say the shahada, is it a genuine expression of your inner tasdiq, or has it become a mere habit? Before your next prayer, pause to renew your intention and feel the weight of those words in your heart.
?
You have learned that actions are not part of iman's definition but fall under Islam. Does this distinction make you take your actions less seriously, or does it remind you that your outward deeds are the proof of your inner faith? What one action will you perfect this week as a sign of your tasdiq?

Intercession of the Prophet and the Righteous
Two verses affirming intercession of Prophet and others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that intercession by Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) and other righteous persons is an established reality in Islam.

Core Idea
Intercession is a promised mercy, not a license to sin.

Ahl al-Sunnah affirm that intercession (shafa'ah) is real and occurs by Allah's permission. The Prophet's intercession is certain, and other righteous individuals also intercede as reported. This passage establishes these beliefs, countering those who deny intercession entirely.

Guided Path
1
Read verses 113-114
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that the Prophet's intercession is described as obligatory (wajib) and given precedence.
The word 'مُقَدَّمًا' suggests his intercession comes first.
2
Identify the two categories of intercessors
Verse 113 mentions the Prophet specifically, while verse 114 mentions 'others from the approved good ones' (مُرْتَضَى الأَخْيَار). These include prophets, angels, and righteous believers.
3
Reflect on the basis of intercession
Intercession is by Allah's permission and for those He approves. It is not automatic for all sinners. This verse assures that intercession is real but conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١١٣ - وَوَاجِبٌ شَفَاعَةُ المُشفّعِ مُحَمَّدٍ مُقَدَّمًا لا تَمْنَعِ ١١٤ - وغيرُهُ من مُرْتَضَى الأَخْيَارِ يَشْفَعُ كما قَدْ جاءَ في الأخبارِ ١١٥ - إِذْ جَائِرٌ غُفْرانُ غير الكُفْرِ فلا نُكَفِّرْ مؤمنًا بالوِزْرِ ١١٦ - وَمَنْ يَمُتْ ولم يَتُبْ مِنْ ذَنْبِهِ فأمرُهُ مُفَوَّضٌ لِرَبِّهِ ١١٧ - وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْذِيبُ بَعْضِ ارْتَكَبْ كبيرةً ثُمَّ الخلودُ مُجْتَنَبْ ١١٨ - وَصِف شَهِيدَ الحَرْبِ بالحياةِ وَرَزْقِهِ مِنْ مُشْتَهى الجنَّاتِ

Commentary
This passage discusses intercession and punishment. Verses 113–114 affirm that the intercession of Prophet Muhammad is obligatory (i.e., a certain reality), and that other righteous persons (مُرْتَضَى الأَخْيَار) also intercede as per reports. Verse 115 clarifies that forgiveness is permissible for sins other than disbelief, and that we do not excommunicate a believer for sin. Verse 116 states that one who dies without repentance is left to God's will. Verse 117 teaches that some major sinners will be punished (in Hell) but not eternally (الخلود مجتنب). Verse 118 instructs us to describe the battlefield martyr as alive and receiving sustenance from Paradise.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe intercession is only for the Prophet or that it is not real. Others think it encourages sin.
Correction: Intercession is real but conditional; it is by Allah's permission and only for those He approves.

A) All prophets

C) The righteous
D) Angels

Intercession is a confirmed reality, with the Prophet's intercession being the foremost, and other righteous also intercede by Allah's permission.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The intercession is for the 'approved good ones' to give. What are you doing today to become one of the 'approved'? Write one concrete action you will take this week to draw closer to that status.
?
If someone told you that intercession means you can sin and still be saved, how would you respond using the very words of this passage? Now ask yourself: Is that my true belief, or is there a part of me that hopes for a free pass?

Forgiveness and the Fate of Sinners
Three verses on forgiveness, deferred judgment, and non-eternal punishment of major sinners.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that forgiveness is available for sins other than disbelief, and that major sins do not necessitate eternal punishment.

Core Idea
Sin does not remove faith; judgment belongs to Allah.

Ahl al-Sunnah hold that major sins do not expel a believer from faith, nor do they guarantee eternal hell. Forgiveness for lesser sins is possible, and unrepentant sinners are left to Allah's will. This passage clarifies that we do not excommunicate for sins and that punishment for major sinners is not eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read verses 115-117
Read the Arabic and translation for verses 115, 116, and 117. Note the key terms: غُفْران (forgiveness), التكفير (excommunication), الوزر (sin), كبيرة (major sin), الخلود (eternity).
2
Understand the principle of forgiveness
Verse 115 says forgiveness is permissible for sins other than disbelief (غير الكفر). This means even major sins can be forgiven if not accompanied by disbelief. Also, we do not declare a believer a disbeliever because of sin.
This refutes Kharijite excommunication of sinners.
3
Comprehend the deferred judgment
Verse 116 states that one who dies without repenting from a sin, his matter is left to Allah: He may forgive or punish. This emphasizes Allah's will, not automatic punishment.
4
Learn about punishment and eternity
Verse 117 says that punishing some major sinners is obligatory (i.e., it will happen), but eternal damnation (الخلود) is avoided. This means no believer will remain in Hell forever.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite view of eternal punishment for major sinners.

Source Text

?

The Living Martyrs of Battle
One verse on the status of battlefield martyrs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that martyrs of battle are spiritually alive and receiving provision in Paradise, as affirmed by Quran and Hadith.

Core Idea
Martyrs are alive, honored, and sustained by Allah.

The Qur'an states that martyrs are alive and provided for by their Lord. This verse instructs us to describe them as such, affirming their special status. The passage counters those who deny the afterlife or trivialize martyrdom.

Guided Path
1
Read verse 118
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the command: 'وَصِف' (describe) – it is obligatory to affirm the martyr's life and provision.
2
Understand the meaning of 'life'
The life is spiritual/barzakh life, not physical. The martyr enjoys pleasures of Paradise in a way unknown to us.
This is based on Quran 3:169.
3
Reflect on the wisdom
This description encourages believers to strive in Allah's cause and removes fear of death for martyrs.

Classification of Sins: Minor and Major
Verse 124 divides sins into minor and major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how sins are divided into minor and major categories in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Sins are two: minor and major; the major requires immediate repentance.

In this verse, the author states the fundamental division of sins. This classification is essential for understanding the rules of repentance and the gravity of different acts.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse
Read verse 124 slowly, noticing the two categories. The author uses 'عندنا' to indicate this is the position of Ahl al-Sunnah.
The dash in the Arabic is a poetic pause.
2
Identify the two types
The verse says sins are two divisions: minor (صغيرة) and major (كبيرة). 'فالثاني' refers to the second category, i.e., major.
Major sins are defined elsewhere as those that carry a specific worldly punishment or are threatened with Hell.
3
Reflect on importance
Why does this distinction matter? Because major sins require immediate repentance, while minor sins can be expiated by good deeds.
This verse sets the stage for the next ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٢٤ - ثمَّ الذُّنُوبُ عِندَنَا قِسْمَانِ - صغيرة كبيرة فالثاني ١٢٥ - منه المتاب واجب في الحالِ ولا انتقاض إنْ يَعُد للحال ١٢٦ - لَكِنْ يجدد توبةً لما اقترف وفي القبولِ رأيهُمْ قَدِ اخْتَلَفُ ١٢٧ - وَحِفْظُ دِينِ ثُمَّ نفسٍ مال نسب ومثلُهَا عَقْلٌ وَعِرْضُ قد وجب
124 - Then sins with us are two divisions: minor and major; so the second (major) 125 - from it, repentance is obligatory immediately, and no invalidation if he returns to the sin later. 126 - But he renews repentance for what he committed, and concerning acceptance, their opinions have differed. 127 - And preservation of religion, then life, property, lineage, and similar to them: intellect and honor, has become obligatory.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of sins and the obligation of repentance, and then the five necessities. Verse 124 states that sins are divided into minor (ṣaghīra) and major (kabīra). Verse 125 declares that repentance is obligatory immediately for a major sin, and that committing the same sin again does not invalidate the previous repentance. Verse 126 notes that one should renew repentance for the sin committed, but scholars differed on whether the repentance is accepted. Verse 127 lists the five necessities (al-ḍarūriyyāt al-khams) that must be preserved: religion (dīn), life (nafs), property (māl), lineage (nasab), and intellect (ʿaql) and honor (ʿirḍ) are added as similar.

Key Terms
الذنوب
الصغيرة
الكبيرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins are the same and repentance is the same for all.
Correction: The verse shows sins are two types with different rulings.

Checkpoint
According to verse 124, how are sins divided?
A) One division
B) Two divisions: minor and major
C) Three divisions
D) No division

Key Takeaway
Sins are two categories: minor and major, and the major type is the focus of the repentance ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your daily actions, do you treat minor sins as insignificant, forgetting that they are still recorded? What minor sin have you been neglecting to repent from, and what step will you take today to seek forgiveness?
?
The passage singles out major sins for immediate attention. Is there a major sin you have delayed repenting from out of pride or procrastination? What is stopping you from turning back to Allah right now?
?
If you truly believed that every sin—big or small—is witnessed and weighed, how would your behavior change in the next hour? Name one specific situation today where you will consciously avoid a minor sin for His sake.

Repentance from Major Sins: Obligation and Renewal
Verses 125-126 on repentance obligation, no invalidation, renewal, and scholarly disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rules of repentance from major sins: immediate obligation, no invalidation upon return, and renewal.

Core Idea
Repentance is obligatory immediately; repeating the sin does not invalidate earlier repentance.

These two verses detail the rules of repentance for major sins. They clarify that repentance must be immediate, that falling back into sin does not nullify it, and that one must renew repentance. The scholars differed on whether it is accepted.

Guided Path
1
Read verses 125-126
Read both verses together. Note the three parts: obligation, no invalidation, renewal.
The verses are a continuation from the previous verse.
2
Examine the obligation
Verse 125 says repentance is obligatory immediately for the major sin. This means one cannot delay.
The Arabic 'في الحال' emphasizes immediacy.
3
Understand no invalidation
If a person commits the same sin again, the previous repentance is not invalidated. 'ولا انتقاض' means no breaking of the repentance.
This is important for hope in Allah's mercy.
4
Note the renewal and disagreement
Verse 126 says one renews repentance for the new sin, but scholars differ on acceptance (of the original repentance or the renewed one? The wording is brief.)
The difference of opinion is noted but not resolved here.

Source Text

?

?

The Five Necessities: Preserving Religion, Life, Property, Lineage, Intellect, and Honor
Verse 127 lists the five necessities (daruriyyat) and adds honor as similar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five necessities (daruriyyat khams) that Islamic law aims to preserve, including the addition of honor (ird).

Core Idea
The Shariah protects five fundamental necessities: religion, life, property, lineage, and intellect.

This verse enumerates the objectives of Islamic law. These are the essential values that must be preserved for human welfare: religion, life, property, lineage, intellect, and honor. The author adds honor (ird) as similar to the five.

Guided Path
1
Read verse 127
Read the verse carefully. It lists five items: religion, life, property, lineage, and then says 'and similar to them: intellect and honor'.
Typically, honor is counted alongside the five.
2
Identify each necessity
Religion (dīn), life (nafs), property (māl), lineage (nasab), and intellect (ʿaql) are the core five. Honor (ʿirḍ) is added as similar.
Some scholars list honor as the sixth.
3
Understand the obligation
The verse says 'قد وجب' meaning preservation of these is obligatory upon the community. This is a fundamental concept in Maqasid al-Shariah.
Preservation includes protecting them from harm.

Source Text

D) Intellect

Key Takeaway
Islamic law obligates preservation of five (or six) fundamental necessities: religion, life, property, lineage, intellect, and honor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what ways are you actively preserving your religion in your daily life, and where might you be neglecting it? Before tomorrow, identify one small but concrete action you can take to strengthen your commitment to your faith.
?
When you consider the preservation of life – not just your own but also others – have you ever taken steps to protect someone's safety or well-being? What would you be willing to sacrifice to save a life if needed?
?
Think about your property and wealth: do you treat them as a trust (amanah) from Allah, or as your own possession to use however you wish? How can you use your wealth today to preserve one of these five necessities for someone in need?

The Ruling on Apostasy: Denying Necessities of Religion
Two verses defining apostasy via denial of necessary knowledge or consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that denying something known by necessity from Islam constitutes apostasy punishable by death as a disbeliever, not as a legal punishment.

Core Idea
Denying a necessary truth of religion is not just a sin; it is apostasy.

These two verses establish the severe ruling for apostasy. They clarify that the punishment is not a hadd (fixed legal penalty) but is for kufr (disbelief). The second verse extends this to denying consensus or permitting what is forbidden.

Guided Path
1
Read the verses carefully
Read the Arabic verses and their translation. Notice the contrast in verse 128: 'kufran laysa hadd' (as a disbeliever, not as a punishment).
Pay attention to the wording 'laysa hadd'—this is a key distinction from fixed penalties.
2
Understand 'known by necessity'
The term 'ma'lum daruratan' refers to matters that are so obvious in Islam that no excuse remains for ignorance. Reflect on examples like the obligation of prayer or the prohibition of zina.
This concept is central to the definition of apostasy in classical jurisprudence.
3
Apply the second verse
Verse 129 extends the same ruling to one who denies a matter of consensus (ijma') or permits a clear prohibition like zina. Consider how this expands the scope of apostasy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
١٢٨ - وَمَنْ لمعلوم ضرورةً جَحَدْ مِنْ ديننا يُقْتَلُ كُفْرًا ليسَ حد ١٢٩ - ومثل هذا مَنْ نَفَى لِمُجْمَعِ أو استباحَ كالزِّنا فلتَسْـ ١٣٠ - وواجب نَصْبُ إمام عدل بالشَّرع فاعلم لا بحكم العقل ١٣١ - فليسَ رُكْنا يُعْتَقَدْ في الدِّينِ ولا تزغ عـن أمـره الـمـبـيـن ١٣٢ - إِلا بِكُفْرٍ فَانْبِدَنَّ عَهْدَهُ فالله يكفينا أذاهُ وَحْدَهُ ١٣٣ - بغير هذا لا يباحُ صَرْفُهُ وليسَ يُعْزَلَ ان يَزُولَ وصفه

Commentary
This passage deals with apostasy and imamate. Verse 128 rules that anyone who denies something known necessarily (ḍarūratan) from Islam is killed as a disbeliever (kufran), not as a legal punishment (ḥadd). Verse 131 clarifies that obedience to the imam is not a creedal pillar, but one should not deviate from his clear command. Verse 132 allows disobeying and abandoning him only if he commands disbelief, and then God suffices. Verse 133 prohibits removing him except in that case; nor is he removed if his description (of justice) disappears (i.e., he becomes unjust).

The Obligation of Appointing a Just Imam and the Limits of Obedience
Four verses on imam appointment, obedience, and conditions for removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that appointing a just imam is an obligation by divine law, not reason, and that disobedience leading to removal is only permitted when the imam commands disbelief.

Core Idea
Obedience to the imam is required except when he commands disbelief.

These verses shift to political leadership. They affirm that the appointment of a just imam is a shar'i obligation, not a rational one. Then they clarify the limits of obedience: one must not deviate from the imam's clear command, but if he commands disbelief, his covenant is broken and he must be removed. Moreover, removal is not permitted for mere loss of justice.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis for imam appointment
Verse 130 states that appointing a just imam is obligatory 'by the law' (bil-shar'), not 'by reason' (bi hukm al-'aql). Reflect on the significance of this distinction in Islamic political thought.
This counters the rationalist view that reason alone dictates the need for a leader.
2
Grasp the nature of obedience
Verse 131 clarifies that obedience to the imam is not a creedal pillar (rukn) but deviation from his clear command is prohibited. Consider what 'clear command' means in practice.
3
Note the exceptions for removal
Verses 132-133 specify that removal is only allowed if the imam commands disbelief (kufr); otherwise, removal is not permitted, even if he loses his description of justice (e.g., becomes unjust). Discuss the implications for political stability.
This is a key point distinguishing Ash'ari political theology.

The Two Types of Sins and the Obligation of Repentance
Sanusi divides sins and states rules for repentance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of sins into major and minor, the immediate obligation to repent from major sins, and the ruling on returning to a sin after repentance.

Core Idea
Sins are either major or minor; repentance from major sins is required at once and must be renewed if repeated.

This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid explains the basic classification of sins and the key rules regarding repentance. It clarifies that repentance from major sins is an immediate duty, and that falling back into sin does not undo the previous repentance but demands a fresh one. The text also notes the scholarly difference over whether repentance is guaranteed acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the concise style of Sanusi: each phrase carries a precise theological ruling.
The text uses 'al-mataab' for repentance, which is a variant spelling of tawba.
2
Identify the two categories of sins
The passage opens by stating sins are two divisions: minor and major. Reflect on what distinguishes them in Islamic theology (major sins are those with a specific threat or punishment).
3
Analyze the three rulings on repentance
From the passage, extract three rulings: (1) Repentance from major sins is obligatory immediately. (2) Returning to the same sin does not revoke the previous repentance. (3) The person must renew repentance for the new sin. Also note the scholars' differing views on whether repentance is necessarily accepted.
The phrase 'qad ikhtalafu' refers to a known theological debate among Ash'aris and others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثمَّ الذُّنُوبُ عِندَنَا قِسْمَانِ صغيرة كبيرة فالثاني منه المتاب واجب في الحالِ ولا انتقاض إنْ يَعُد للحال لَكِنْ يجدد توبةً لما اقترف وفي القبولِ رأيهُمْ قَدِ اخْتَلَفُ
Then sins according to us are two divisions: minor and major. The second (i.e., major) - repentance from it is obligatory immediately, and there is no revocation if he returns to the same sin, but he renews a repentance for what he committed. And regarding acceptance, their opinions have differed.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage classifies sins (dhunub) into two types: minor (saghira) and major (kabira). It then states that repentance (tawba) from major sins is obligatory immediately, without delay. If the person returns to the same sin after repenting, the previous repentance is not invalidated (la intiqad), but he must renew repentance for the new sin. Finally, it notes that scholars have differed on whether repentance is necessarily accepted by God (in terms of being sure of acceptance) – a reference to the theological debate on whether God is obliged to accept repentance.

Key Terms
الذُّنُوب (sins)
قِسْمَان (two divisions)
صغيرة (minor)
كبيرة (major)
المتاب (repentance)
انتقاض (revocation)
القبول (acceptance)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that if you repent from a major sin but commit it again, your first repentance becomes invalid and you must repent anew from scratch, as if the first never happened.
Correction: The text explicitly states 'la intiqad' — the first repentance is not revoked. It remains valid and accepted; only the new sin requires a fresh repentance.

Checkpoint

A) The original repentance is revoked
B) The person must renew repentance
C) The person is not considered a sinner
D) The person is permanently forgiven

Key Takeaway
Sins are divided into major and minor; repentance from major sins is obligatory immediately, remains valid even if repeated, and must be renewed for each new sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever delayed repenting from a major sin, telling yourself you will do it later? How does knowing that repentance is obligatory immediately change what you must do right now?
?
If you have repented from a sin but then fell into it again, do you feel that your previous repentance was worthless? According to this lesson, it remains valid. Can you truly accept that and still renew your repentance without despair?
?
The scholars differ on whether Allah necessarily accepts repentance. Does that uncertainty cause you to rely more on your own deeds or on Allah's mercy? How should it affect your attitude when you repent tonight?

Apostasy Through Denial of Essential Doctrines
Ruling on apostasy for denying essential matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ruling on apostasy for denying matters known by necessity or consensus, and the distinction between execution for disbelief and a fixed legal punishment.

Core Idea
Denying foundational religious truths is apostasy, not a mere legal offence.

This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid commentary addresses a critical aspect of Islamic law: the ruling on one who denies essential doctrines of the religion. It clarifies that such denial is considered apostasy, carrying the death penalty as a measure against disbelief, not as a prescribed hadd punishment. The text also extends this ruling to denying matters of consensus or deeming clearly forbidden acts permissible.

Guided Path
1
Read the original Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once carefully, noting the key terms. Pay attention to the structure: two main cases are mentioned.
The OCR has a slight truncation at the end; the intended meaning is clear.
2
Understand the first case: denial of known-by-necessity
Identify what 'known by necessity' means – doctrines so fundamental that any Muslim must know them. The punishment is death for disbelief, not a hadd.
3
Understand the second case: denial of consensus or deeming permissible
The same ruling applies to one who rejects a matter of consensus or considers something clearly forbidden (like adultery) as permissible.
This shows the range of what constitutes apostasy.
4
Reflect on the distinction between disbelief punishment and hadd
The text explicitly says 'not a fixed punishment'. This distinguishes apostasy from crimes like theft or fornication which have prescribed penalties.
The death here is for disbelief itself, not as a legal penalty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ لمعلوم ضرورةً جَحَدْ مِنْ ديننا يُقْتَلُ كُفْرًا ليسَ حدْ ومثل هذا مَنْ نَفَى لِمُجْمَعِ أو استباحَ كالزِّنا فلتَسْـ
And whoever denies what is known by necessity from our religion is killed as disbelief, not as a fixed punishment. And like this is one who denies a matter of consensus or deems permissible like adultery, then let him...
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage deals with apostasy (ridda) and the punishment for denying essential doctrines of Islam. It states that whoever denies (jahada) something that is known by necessity (ma'lum bi-darura) from the religion is to be killed as a disbeliever, not as a fixed punishment (hadd) – meaning it is for apostasy, not for a prescribed hadd crime. Similarly, one who denies a matter of consensus (mujma') or deems permissible (istabaha) something clearly forbidden like adultery (zina) falls under the same ruling. The text emphasizes that the death penalty is for disbelief, not as a fixed legal penalty.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any denial of a religious opinion or scholarly view leads to apostasy and the death penalty.
Correction: The text specifies that only denial of matters known by necessity or matters of consensus constitutes apostasy. Not every disputed issue reaches that level.

Key Takeaway

Concluding Prayers for Sincerity and Salvation
The poet concludes with personal supplications and salawat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the supplications made by the poet at the end of Jawharat al-Tawhid, covering sincerity, deliverance, proof on the Day of Questioning, and salawat upon the Prophet.

Core Idea
The poem closes with humble hopes and prayers, seeking sincerity, salvation, and divine proof on the Day of Questioning.

The poet concludes his theological exposition not with a summary but with a series of personal supplications. These lines reveal his deep reliance on Allah for sincerity, protection from hypocrisy and desires, and for a clear proof on the Day of Questioning. He then sends prayers upon the Prophet, his family, and his followers. This lesson unpacks these closing prayers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the repetition of 'arju' (I hope) and the shift from supplication to salawat.
The poet uses a personal, humble tone.
2
Identify the specific requests
List the four things the poet hopes for from Allah: sincerity from hypocrisy, deliverance, protection from self and vain desire, and a proof at the time of questioning.
These are all spiritual gifts, not worldly.
3
Understand the warning
The poet says 'whoever inclines to these has strayed.' Determine what 'these' refers to: hypocrisy, the self, and vain desire. Why are they dangerous?
This shows the poet's awareness of internal spiritual threats.
4
Appreciate the salawat
Read the salawat on the Prophet, who is described as 'whose habit is mercy.' Reflect on why mercy is highlighted here and how it connects to the previous supplications.
The Prophet's mercy is a source of hope for sinners.
5
Connect to personal practice
Consider how these prayers can be part of a believer's daily supplication. What does this conclusion teach about the relationship between theology and personal devotion?
Theology is meant to lead to humble reliance on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا وَأَرْجُو الله في الإِخْلَاصِ من الرياء ثم في الخَلاصِ من الرَّحِيمِ ثُمَّ نَفْسِي والهَوَى فمَنْ يَمِلْ لهؤلاء قد غوى هَذَا وَأَرْجُو الله أن يمنَحَنَا عندَ السُّؤَالِ مُطْلَقًا حُجَّتَنا ثمَّ الصَّلاةُ والسَّلامُ الدَّائم على نَبِيِّ دأبه المراحم محمَّدٍ وَصَحْبه وعِتْرَتِهُ وتابع لنهجه من امنة
This, and I hope from Allah sincerity from hypocrisy, then deliverance from the Merciful, then (from) my self and vain desire; for whoever inclines to these has strayed. This, and I hope from Allah that He grants us at the questioning absolutely our proof. Then the prayer and eternal peace upon the Prophet whose habit is mercy, Muhammad, and his Companions and his family, and a follower of his path from among those who are safe.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage concludes the poem with a series of personal supplications and prayers. The poet expresses hope (arju) from Allah for several things: first, sincerity (ikhlas) and protection from hypocrisy (riya'), then deliverance (khalas) from the Merciful, and then protection from his own self (nafs) and vain desires (hawa). He warns that whoever inclines to these (hypocrisy, self, desires) has gone astray. He again hopes that Allah grants him a clear proof (hujja) on the Day of Questioning (when questioned in the grave or on the Day of Judgment). Finally, he sends eternal prayers and peace (salat wa salam da'im) upon the Prophet Muhammad, whose constant practice was mercy (marahim), and upon his Companions, his family, and those who follow his path and are safe.

عترته (his family)
تابع (follower)
نهجه (his path)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'at the questioning' (عند السؤال) refers only to the questioning in the grave.
Correction: The poet uses the word 'mutlaqan' (absolutely/generally), indicating that the request for proof applies to all forms of questioning, including the grave and the Day of Judgment.

Checkpoint

A) Wealth
B) High status
C) His proof (hujja)
D) Forgiveness

Key Takeaway
The conclusion of Jawharat al-Tawhid emphasizes that theological knowledge must be accompanied by sincere reliance on Allah and prayers for guidance, protection, and proof on the Day of Questioning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The poet prays for sincerity from hypocrisy. Look at your own worship today: is there any action you did seeking praise or recognition from others rather than Allah? What will you do to purify that intention right now?
?
The poet asks deliverance from his own self and vain desires. Which desire in your life is currently pulling you away from obedience to Allah? Be specific—what is one small step you can take today to resist it?
?
Imagine standing before Allah at the questioning. What proof (hujja) will you present? Are you investing in the deeds and knowledge that will serve as your proof? Write down one concrete action you will start tomorrow to build that proof.

Hope for Sincerity and Protection from Self and Desire
The poet expresses hope for sincerity and protection from self and desire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive nature of sincerity (ikhlas) as encompassing protection from show-off (riya') and from the inclinations of the soul and desire.

Core Idea
Sincerity requires deliverance from both outward show-off and inward desires.

In these two lines, the poet turns from affirming theological doctrines to making a personal plea to Allah. He hopes for sincerity and deliverance from three internal enemies: riya' (show-off), the self (nafs), and desire (hawa). This lesson explores the meaning of this prayer and the warning it contains against inclining to these harmful tendencies.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic lines
Read the two lines of poetry slowly, noticing the keywords: إخلاص, رياء, نفس, هوى. The poetry is in rajaz meter.
2
Identify the poet's two hopes
The poet hopes for sincerity (الإخلاص) from Allah, and for deliverance (الخلاص) from riya', then from the self and desire.
Sincerity and deliverance are two aspects of the same struggle.
3
Understand the warning
The poet warns that whoever inclines (يمل) to these (the self and desire) has gone astray (قد غوى). The word 'غوى' implies deviation from truth.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Sincerity is not a one-time act but a continuous struggle against both outward hypocrisy (riya') and inward desires (hawa).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا وَأَرْجُو الله في الإِخْلَاصِ من الرياء ثم في الخَلاصِ
من الرَّحِيمِ ثُمَّ نَفْسِي والهَوَى فمَنْ يَمِلْ لهؤلاء قد غوى
This, and I hope from Allah sincerity, from show-off, then in deliverance. From the Merciful, then my soul and desire - so whoever inclines to these has gone astray.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
The poet states his hope (أرجو) from Allah for sincerity (الإخلاص) and deliverance from riya' (الرياء) and then from one's own soul and desires (نفسي والهوى). He declares that whoever inclines to these (soul and desire) has gone astray (قد غوى). This is a personal prayer and a warning against internal enemies of faith.

Key Terms
إخلاص
رياء
هوى
غوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sincerity (ikhlas) only means avoiding show-off (riya') in acts of worship.
Correction: Sincerity also requires deliverance from the inclinations of the soul (nafs) and desire (hawa), as the poet includes them as enemies alongside riya'.

Checkpoint
What does the poet hope for from Allah in this passage?
A) Wealth and children
B) Victory over enemies
C) Sincerity and deliverance from show-off
D) Paradise

Key Takeaway
Sincerity requires protection from both outward show-off and inward self and desire, and inclining to these leads to misguidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform a good deed, who are you really trying to please? Are you honest enough to admit when the thought of others' approval creeps in?
?
Your soul and desires whisper constantly. Can you identify one desire that you have followed today that you know leads you away from Allah's pleasure? What will you do to resist it tomorrow?
?
The poet ends with a warning: inclining to these leads to misguidance. If you were to evaluate your life right now, are you inclining toward sincerity or toward your own desires? What is one concrete change you will make this week to shift that balance?

Ruling on the Muqallid: Is Blind Following Accepted in Creed?
Classification of scholarly opinions on the muqallid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing scholarly opinions on the faith and sinfulness of a person who blindly follows creedal beliefs (taqlīd).

Core Idea
Faith through blind imitation is valid for those unable to reflect, but sinful for those who can.

This lesson explores a nuanced issue in Islamic theology: the status of a person who accepts creedal doctrines by blind imitation (taqlīd) without independent proof. Scholars have debated whether such a person is a believer or a disbeliever, and whether they are sinful. The passage presents five distinct views and gives preference to one based on the capacity for reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English. Notice the range of opinions mentioned.
The passage is a classification.
2
Identify the five views
List the five opinions as presented in the passage. Note which one the author prefers and why.
The key factor is ability to perform naẓar (reflection).
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider why the majority view distinguishes based on ability. How does this relate to the principle that Allah does not burden a soul beyond its capacity?
This connects to broader theological principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا مَن حفظ العقائد بالتقليد فقد اختلف العلماء فيه : أمؤمن هو أم كافرُ ؟ وإذا كان مؤمناً فهل هو عاص باكتفائه بالتقليد أم غير عاص ؟ والذي صححه الجمهرة من العلماء: أنَّه مؤمن ، ثمَّ إن كان بحيث يقدر على النظر فهو عاص بتركه ما قدر عليه، وإن كان بحيث لا يقدر على النظر فهو غير عاص لأنَّه ما ترك شيئًا إلا وهو عاجز عن تحصيله؛ وقال جماعة: هو مؤمن غير عاص مطلقا ، أي : سواء أقدر على النظر أم لم يقدر ، وقال قوم: هو مؤمن عاص مطلقا ، وقيل: هو كافر، وعليه جرى السنوسي في شرح الكبرى ونصره، وشنّع على من قال: إن التقليد كافٍ ، ويقال : إنه رجع إلى القول بكفاية التقليد، والذي تطمئن إليه النَّفس هو القول الأول من هذه الأقوال ؛ لأنَّ مِن العوام مَن لا يستطيع فهم هذه الأدلة ، ولأنَّ الرَّسول كان يقبل من كلِّ واحدٍ ما قدر عليه. ولم يُعرف عنه التشدد في حمل الناس على إدراك ما لم يمنحهم الله القدرة على إدراكه ، وسيأتي لهذا الكلام تتمة عند إثارة هذه المسألة في كلام المصنف.
As for one who preserves the creeds by blind following (taqlīd), the scholars have differed concerning him: Is he a believer or a disbeliever? And if he is a believer, is he sinful due to his being content with taqlīd or not sinful? The view that the majority of scholars have declared correct is that he is a believer. Then, if he is able to engage in reflection (naẓar), he is sinful for abandoning what he was capable of. But if he is not able to reflect, then he is not sinful, because he did not abandon anything except that he was incapable of acquiring it. A group said: He is a believer and not sinful absolutely, i.e. regardless of whether he is able to reflect or not. Another group said: He is a believer and sinful absolutely. And it was said: He is a disbeliever, and this is the view that al-Sanūsī followed in his commentary al-Kubrā and supported, and he severely criticized those who said that taqlīd is sufficient. It is said that he later retracted to the view that taqlīd is sufficient. The view that the soul feels at ease with is the first of these opinions, because among the common people are those who cannot understand these proofs, and because the Prophet (ﷺ) accepted from each person what he was capable of. He is not known to have been strict in burdening people to grasp what Allah had not granted them the ability to grasp. There will be a continuation of this discussion when this issue is raised in the author's text.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of scholarly opinions on the muqallid—one who accepts creedal beliefs by blind imitation without proof. The author outlines five views: (1) He is a believer, and if capable of reflection (naẓar) he is sinful for not using it; if incapable, not sinful. This is the majority view. (2) He is a believer and not sinful at all. (3) He is a believer and sinful in all cases. (4) He is a disbeliever. The author notes that al-Sanūsī held the disbeliever view in his commentary al-Kubrā but later possibly retracted. The author then states his preference: the first view, because many common people cannot understand proofs, and the Prophet accepted people based on their capacity without undue strictness.

Key Terms
تقليد
نظر
مقلد
كافر
عاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that blind imitation (taqlīd) is either always sufficient for faith or always makes one a disbeliever.
Correction: The correct view distinguishes based on the individual's ability to perform reflection (naẓar): if capable and they do not reflect, they are sinful; if incapable, not sinful. Faith itself is valid through taqlīd according to the majority.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view, what determines whether a muqallid is sinful?
A) His sincerity of faith
B) His ability to perform reflection (naẓar)
C) His knowledge of detailed proofs
D) His adherence to a particular school

Key Takeaway
The muqallid's faith is valid according to the majority, but sinfulness depends on their ability to engage in reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you rely on taqlīd in matters of creed without seeking understanding? Where do you stand in your own heart regarding the proofs of your faith?
?
If you are capable of deeper reflection (naẓar) but neglect it, are you comfortable with the sin of abandoning what you are able to do?
?
How does the Prophet's gentleness with capacity challenge your own attitude towards those who struggle with doctrine?

Obligation of Knowing God and Exemption for Insanity
Al-Laqqani states the obligation and an exemption condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the obligation to know God applies only to the morally responsible (mukallaf), and that insanity before puberty removes this responsibility.

Core Idea
Insanity before puberty lifts the obligation to know God.

Al-Laqqani establishes a foundational principle: knowledge of God is obligatory upon every mukallaf. However, he immediately qualifies this by noting that if a person becomes insane before reaching puberty, that obligation is removed. This raises important questions about accountability and divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic phrase
Read the Arabic excerpt carefully once or twice. Notice the key terms: 'mukallaf' and 'junun' (insanity).
The phrase is concise; don't rush.
2
Identify the condition
The text states a conditional: if insanity occurs before puberty, then the obligation is lifted. Understand that 'mukallaf' implies being accountable, but this condition suspends that accountability.
This is a specific exception in Islamic law.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this exception exists. It shows that God does not burden a person beyond their capacity, as the insane lack rational discernment.
This relates to divine justice and mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوب معرفة الله على المكلف طَراً عليه الجُنونُ قبل أن يبلُغَ ونحو ذلك ، أنَّ كلَّا منهم يُدلي بحجةٍ ويقول: عَقَلْتُ أو ذُكِّرْتُ الآمنتُ ، فترفع لهم نارٌ ، ويقال لهم: ادخلوها ، فمَن دخلها كانت عليه بردًا وسلاما ، ومَن امتنع أُدْخِلها كُرها. والمراد بالأكمه : الذي لا يدري أين يتوجه ، وهو الأحمق والمعتوه المُصرَّحُ به في الحديث ، والله أعلم.
The obligation of knowing God upon the morally responsible person, if insanity occurs upon him before he reaches puberty and similar [cases], that each of them will present an argument and say: 'I reasoned' or 'I was reminded and I believed', so a fire will be raised for them, and it will be said to them: 'Enter it.' Whoever enters it, it will be coolness and safety upon him, and whoever refuses, they will be made to enter it by force. And the meaning of 'blind' (al-akmah) is: he who does not know where to go, and he is the idiot and the imbecile explicitly mentioned in the hadith. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal discussion about the obligation of knowing God and the eschatological testing of those who never received divine revelation. It begins by stating that the obligation to know God applies to the morally responsible person (mukallaf). It then addresses a scenario where a person becomes insane before reaching puberty, implying that such a person is not held accountable in the same way. The passage then cites a hadith about a group of people who were never reached by a prophet: they will be given an argument and will say they reasoned or believed when reminded. Then a fire will be shown to them, and they are commanded to enter it. Those who enter find it cool and safe; those who refuse are forced in. This is part of the testing for those who lived in the 'interval' (fatra) between prophets. The author then clarifies the term 'blind' (akmah) in the hadith, explaining it refers to someone who lacks guidance and cannot distinguish directions, i.e., the mentally deficient who have no discernment. This is the intended meaning, not necessarily physical blindness.

Key Terms
مكلف
جنون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Everyone, regardless of mental state, is obligated to know God and will be punished for ignorance.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that insanity before puberty (and similar conditions) lifts the obligation, showing that accountability is conditional upon sound reason.

Checkpoint
What condition does Al-Laqqani mention that removes the obligation to know God?
A) Reaching old age
B) Insanity before puberty
C) Living in a non-Muslim country
D) Having no teacher

Key Takeaway
Insanity before puberty removes the obligation to know God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever taken for granted your ability to reason and know God? What would it mean for you if that ability were lost before you even had the chance to seek Him?
?
If you were to lose your sanity tomorrow, would your current knowledge of God and your obligations be enough to secure your account before Him?
?
Knowing that accountability is tied to rational capacity, how urgently should you use your present reason to fulfill what is required of you before it is too late?

Testing of the Unreached and Clarification of 'Akmah'
Al-Laqqani narrates the hadith of testing and defines a key term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hadith about the eschatological testing of those who never received divine revelation, and the meaning of 'blind' (al-akmah) in that context.

Core Idea
Those untouched by revelation will be tested with a fire in the afterlife.

Al-Laqqani cites a profound hadith about a group of people who were never reached by any prophet. In the afterlife, they will be presented with a fire as a test, and their response determines their fate. He then clarifies a key term in the narration to ensure correct understanding.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith
Read the Arabic narration and its translation. Notice the sequence: they present an argument, a fire is raised, they are told to enter, and the outcome differs based on obedience.
This is a well-known hadith about the 'fatra' (interval).
2
Understand the test
The test is a fire: entering it is a trial of faith. The text says for the obedient it becomes cool and safe, while the disobedient are forced in. This shows that divine justice accounts for those who lacked prophetic guidance.
Contrast with literal punishment.
3
Clarify 'akmah'
Al-Laqqani explains that 'al-akmah' (blind) in the hadith refers not to physical blindness but to mental deficiency: one who does not know where to go, i.e., an idiot or imbecile. This ensures the term is understood correctly.
This reflects careful linguistic and theological analysis.

Whoever enters it, it will be coolness and safety upon him, and whoever refuses, they will be made to enter it by force. The Faith of the Muqallid: Taqlid and Tardid
Defines taqlid and interprets the term tardid in the matn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of taqlid, the meaning of tardid in the context of a muqallid's faith, and the scholarly disagreement about its validity.

Core Idea
Blind faith in tawhid is never completely free from uncertainty, though scholars differ on what that uncertainty means.

This passage addresses a critical question: has a person truly believed in tawhid if they merely follow authority without understanding the evidence? The commentator unpacks a dense line of the matn to define taqlid and clarify the nature of the uncertainty (tardid) that afflicts such faith. Understanding this will help you appreciate why kalam emphasizes rational proof for foundational beliefs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the dense commentary style and the focus on the term 'tardid'.
2
Define taqlid as presented
The commentator defines taqlid as accepting another's statement without knowledge of its evidence. Contrast this with reasoned belief.
3
Analyze the interpretations of tardid
Identify the three possible meanings of 'tardid': scholarly disagreement, personal confusion, or potential future doubt. Note the commentator's preferred interpretation.
Read carefully how each interpretation is argued.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the commentator favors the interpretation of tardid as ikhtilaf al-ulama. How does this shape the ruling on the muqallid's faith?
Think about the role of evidence in faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم إيمان المقلد، وبيان الخلاف الحاصل فيه
إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ في التَّوْحِيدِ إيمَانُهُ لَمْ يَخْلُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدِ
ثمَّ علل وجوبَ المعرفة السابقة بقوله : (إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ) (1) أي: إِنَّما أوجبنا معرفة ما ذُكر (2) بالدليل لأنَّه متى كانَ مُتأهلا (3) لفهم البراهين المكلّف معرفة هو - كلف (1) هذا الكلام - كما قال الشارح - تعليل لوجوب المعرفة الذي ذكره بقوله: «فكل من حتما وجبا عليه أن يعرف – إلى آخر» فكأنَّ المصنف قد قال: وإنما وجب شرعاً على المكلف معرفة ما ذكرنا ؛ لأنَّ مَن قلَّد إلى آخر. فـ(إذ) في كلامه: حرف دال على التعليل، والتقليد: أن تأخذ بقول غيرك من غير أن تعرف دليل هذا القول ، فإذا عرفت الدليل الذي استند إليه صاحب القول الذي أخذت به لم تكن مقلدا ، والمراد بإيمانه في قوله: «إيمانه لم يخل من ترديد تصديقه التابع لجزمه بأحكام التوحيد من غير دليل، وأحسنُ ما يحمل عليه (الترديد) في هذه العبارة أن يكون المراد به اختلاف العلماء فيه على الوجوه التي ذكرها الشارح وسنذكرها تفصيلا ، وعلى هذا يكون ما يأتي في كلام المصنّف بعد ذلك تفسيرا لهذا المجمَل ، وهذا خيرٌ مِن أن يحمل (الترديد) على التحير من نفس المكلف ؛ فإن ظاهره يفيد الجزم الذي عبر عنه بقوله: «إيمانه» يجامعُ التَّحير والتَّردُّد، مع أَنَّ المعلوم أن الإنسان متى كان جازماً بالشيء لم يكن عنده تردد أصلا ، وإن أمكن الجواب عن هذا بأنَّ في الكلام مضافًا محذوفًا، والتقدير: إيمانه لم يخل عن قبول ترديد، يعني أنه جازم الآن، ولكنه لعدم معرفته بالدليل لا يؤمن أن يطرأ عليه الترديد، ولا شكّ أنَّ ما لا يُحْوج إلى الاعتراض والجواب أولى أن يؤخذ به محي.
(2) قوله : ( ما ذكر أي من الواجب والمستحيل والجائز في حق الله وحق رسله. الشنواني . ) : (3) قوله : (لأنه متى كان ... إلخ) هذا التعليل مضر ؛ لأنه يقتضي أن الخلاف إنما هو فيمن عنده أهلية وليس كذلك، بل هو مطلق، فالأولى حذفه ؛ لأن بعض الأقوال الآتية يطلق وبعضها=
The ruling on the faith of the muqallid (one who blindly follows) and explanation of the disagreement concerning it. 'Since everyone who follows blindly in tawhid, his faith is not free from uncertainty (tardid).' Then he gave the reason for the obligation of previous knowledge by his statement: 'Since everyone who...' (1) i.e., we only obligated knowledge of what was mentioned (2) by evidence because when he is capable (3) of understanding proofs... the mukallaf knows... (1) This statement – as the commentator said – is a reason for the obligation of knowledge that he mentioned in his statement: 'So everyone who is necessarily obligated to know – to the end.' So it is as if the author said: 'And it is only obligated by law upon the mukallaf to know what we mentioned, because whoever follows blindly... to the end.' So (idh) in his statement is a particle indicating causation. And taqlid is that you take the statement of another without knowing the evidence for that statement. When you know the evidence upon which the holder of the statement you took is based, you are not a muqallid. And what is meant by 'his faith' in his statement: 'His faith is not free from tardid' is his belief (tasdiq) that follows his certainty (jazzm) in the rulings of tawhid without evidence. And the best interpretation of (tardid) in this expression is that what is meant is the disagreement of scholars concerning it in the ways the commentator mentioned and we will mention in detail. On this basis, what comes in the author's speech after that is an explanation of this summarized statement. This is better than interpreting (tardid) as confusion (tahayyur) from the mukallaf himself; for its apparent meaning gives certainty (jazzm) which he expressed by 'his faith' that coexists with confusion and indecision, while it is known that when a person is certain of something, he has no indecision at all. Even if an answer to this is possible by saying that in the speech there is a deleted genitive construction, and the estimation is: 'His faith is not free from acceptance of tardid', meaning that he is certain now, but due to his lack of knowledge of the evidence, he is not safe from tardid occurring to him. And there is no doubt that what does not require objection and answer is more worthy to be taken. Muhyi. (2) His statement: 'What was mentioned' i.e., from the wajib, mustahil, and ja'iz in the right of Allah and the right of His messengers. Al-Shanwani. (3) His statement: 'Because when he is capable... etc.' This causation is harmful (mudir) because it implies that the disagreement is only concerning the one who has capacity, but it is not so; rather it is absolute. So it is better to delete it because some of the following statements are absolute and some=
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn line: 'Since everyone who follows blindly in tawhid, his faith is not free from uncertainty (tardid).' It begins by stating the topic: the ruling on the faith of the muqallid and the disagreement concerning it. The commentator explains that this line is a reason (ta'lil) for the earlier obligation of knowledge. The word 'idh' is a particle indicating causation. Then the meaning of taqlid is defined: taking another's statement without knowing its evidence; if you know the evidence, you are not a muqallid. The commentator then interprets 'tardid' (uncertainty) in the matn. He suggests it does not mean confusion in the muqallid's own mind, but rather the existence of scholarly disagreement (ikhtilaf al-'ulama) about the validity of such faith. The author's subsequent speech will explain this summarized statement. He argues against interpreting 'tardid' as the muqallid's personal doubt because the outward meaning of 'his faith' implies certainty, which cannot coexist with doubt. An alternative interpretation is given: a deleted genitive construction, meaning 'his faith is not free from acceptance of tardid,' i.e., while he is currently certain, he is vulnerable to future doubt due to lack of evidence. The commentator prefers the interpretation referring to scholarly disagreement. The passage ends with notes from al-Shanwani defining 'what was mentioned' and criticizing one of the causative statements for being harmful (mudir) because it restricts the disagreement to those with capacity, whereas it is absolute. The text cuts off mid-sentence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That taqlid is always permissible and acceptable in matters of tawhid.
Correction: The passage shows that taqlid in tawhid leads to uncertainty (tardid), and scholars disagree about its validity. For those capable of reasoning, it is not acceptable.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the best interpretation of 'tardid' in the matn 'his faith is not free from tardid'?
A) The muqallid's own confusion and indecision.
B) The disagreement among scholars about the validity of the muqallid's faith.
C) The possibility of future doubt due to lack of evidence.
D) The muqallid's certainty in his faith.

Key Takeaway
The faith of a muqallid in tawhid is subject to uncertainty, understood by scholars as the existence of disagreement about its validity rather than personal doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever inherited your belief in tawhid without examining the evidence yourself? According to this lesson, what is the risk of such blind acceptance? Would you be able to defend your faith with clear proofs if questioned today?
?
When you recite 'La ilaha illa Allah', do you do so with certainty (jazzm) or with hidden doubts you've never confronted? This lesson shows that even the muqallid's faith is considered uncertain by scholars. How certain are you that your tawhid is not merely taqlid?
?
Are you willing to test your own iman by asking yourself: 'Do I believe in Allah because I have reasoned to His existence, or because I was raised to? If a strong argument against tawhid were presented to you, could you refute it using evidence?' Let this lesson push you to seek knowledge until your faith is built on proof, not tradition.
