The Ruling on the Faith of the Muqallid
Commentary on the dispute over taqlid in creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement over the faith of the muqallid and the relied-upon view that he is a believer but sinful if capable of investigation.

Core Idea
The muqallid is a believer but disobedient, not a disbeliever.

This passage from the commentary addresses a key dispute in creed: Is blind acceptance (taqlid) sufficient for faith in matters where ignorance is disbelief? The commentator clarifies the locus of disagreement—distinguishing between attributes—and presents two interpretations of the majority position. By studying this, you will see how scholars carefully balance the requirements of faith with the capacities of laypeople.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once to get the overall discussion. Notice the references to different scholars and the use of key terms like 'muqallid' and 'taqlid'.
The passage is a commentary explaining a line from a poem.
2
Identify the locus of disagreement
Look for the phrase 'موضع الخلاف' (locus of disagreement). The passage states that the dispute concerns taqlid in matters whose ignorance is kufr, such as sifāt al-salūb and al-maʿnawiyya, but not matters like sifāt al-maʿānī.
This distinction is crucial for understanding the scope of the debate.
3
Understand the two interpretations of 'non-sufficiency'
The phrase 'عدم الاكتفاء بالتقليد' (non-sufficiency of taqlid) can mean either that the faith is invalid (muqallid is kafir) or that taqlid is impermissible (muqallid is sinful). The footnote explains both possibilities.
Al-Sanusi held the first view, but the relied-upon view is the second.
4
Recognize the relied-upon view
The passage concludes that the muqallid is a believer who is disobedient if he had the capacity for investigation. This differentiates from the view of mere impermissibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعدمها (۱) (بَعْضُ القَوْمِ) المصنِّفين في هذا الفنّ (يَحْكِي الخُلْفا) أي الخلافَ أهله عن من المتقدمين والمتأخرين . (۱) قوله (صحة إيمانه يندرج تحت هذا محرم النظر . واعلم أن موضع الخلاف التقليد فيما جهله كفر ، كصفات السلوب والمعنوية. أما صفات المعاني ونحوها مما لا يكفر منكرها فلا ؛ كما أفاده العلامة الملوي . الأمير. والأستاذ (۱) وإمام الحرمين (۲) والجمهور : عدم الاكتفاء بالتقليد (۳) في الدينية ، وعُزي للإمام مالك . (۳) قوله: (عدم الاكتفاء بالتقليد وهو صادق بأمرين وهما: عدم الصحة وعدم جواز التقليد، الصحة لكن الظاهر عدم بمعنى أن المقلد كافر وعليه السنوسي في الكبرى، لكن المعتمد أنه مؤمن عاص إن كان فيه أهلية النظر ، ليغاير قوله الآتي: (ومنهم من نقل عن الجمهور عدم جواز التقليد الشنواني .
And its absence. (1) Some of the people, the authors in this art, relate the dispute, i.e., the disagreement about it from the earlier and later scholars. (1) His saying: 'The soundness of his faith falls under this forbidden consideration. And know that the locus of disagreement is taqlid in what is ignorance of which is disbelief, like the attributes of negation and the attributed-to-God meanings. As for the attributes of meanings and the like, whose denier does not become a disbeliever, then no; as the scholar al-Mallawi indicated. Al-Amir.' And al-Ustadh (1) and Imam al-Haramayn (2) and the majority: that taqlid is not sufficient in religious beliefs, and it is attributed to Imam Malik. (3) His saying: 'The non-sufficiency of taqlid, and it applies to two things: the lack of validity and the lack of permissibility of taqlid, but the apparent is non-sufficiency meaning that the muqallid is a disbeliever, and upon this is al-Sanusi in al-Kubra, but the relied-upon is that he is a believer who is disobedient if he has the capacity for investigation, to differentiate from his upcoming saying: 'And among them are those who related from the majority the non-permissibility of taqlid.' Al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining a line from the poem about the ruling on the faith of the muqallid (one who follows blindly in creed). The commentator first quotes the line: 'And its absence. Some of the people, the authors in this art, relate the dispute...' The footnote (1) expands on this: the 'absence' refers to the absence of soundness of the muqallid's faith, and the disputed point is taqlid regarding matters whose ignorance constitutes disbelief, such as attributes of negation (sifāt al-salūb) and attributes that are essential to God (al-maʿnawiyya). However, matters like attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) are not included because denying them does not make one a disbeliever. Then the commentator adds that al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini), Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), and the majority hold that taqlid is not sufficient in religious beliefs, and this is attributed to Imam Malik. The second footnote (3) clarifies: 'non-sufficiency of taqlid' can mean either that the muqallid's faith is invalid (he is a disbeliever) or that taqlid is impermissible (he is a sinner). Al-Sanusi in his al-Kubra holds the first view, but the relied-upon position is that the muqallid is a believer but sinful if he had the capacity to investigate. This distinguishes it from another view that taqlid is simply impermissible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the majority hold that taqlid is sufficient.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the majority hold taqlid is not sufficient, though they differ on whether it leads to disbelief or sin.

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view mentioned in the passage, what is the status of a muqallid who has the capacity for investigation?
A) He is a disbeliever.
B) He is a believer and righteous.
C) He is a believer but disobedient.
D) His faith is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon view is that the muqallid who has the capacity for investigation is a believer but sinful, not a disbeliever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you accept your beliefs purely because you were raised in them, or have you actively investigated the proofs for God's existence and attributes? The muqallid is considered sinful if he had the capacity to investigate—are you excusing yourself from the duty of knowing why you believe?
?
If you were to be asked on the Day of Judgment about your belief in God's attributes, would you only be able to say 'I followed my parents' or 'I followed my teacher'? This lesson warns that such blind following without investigation is disobedience. What are you doing today to ensure your faith is based on certain knowledge?
?
The relied-upon view says the muqallid is a believer but sinful. Does this comfort you, or does it challenge you to move beyond taqlid? How can you, starting this week, deepen your understanding of a single attribute of God so that your faith is no longer merely inherited?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner

The First Position: Muqallid as Believer but Sinful
Opening statement and first position on the muqallid's faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first position on the faith of the muqallid: that he is a believer, yet sinful for abandoning the knowledge that correct reasoning produces.

Core Idea
Taqlid in beliefs is not permissible, but the imitator may still be a believer.

The discussion begins with a clear assertion: taqlīd (imitation) in religious beliefs is not permissible according to the majority. Yet, a key question arises: does this impermissibility mean the imitator loses his faith? The first position answers that he remains a believer, but is sinful for neglecting the duty of attaining knowledge through correct reasoning. This lesson focuses on that first view and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal English translation. Notice the strong opening: the ruling on the muqallid's faith is introduced with a prohibition of taqlid in beliefs.
The passage includes a footnote marker (1) that refers to a gloss explaining the meaning of 'non-permissibility'.
2
Identify the first position
Identify the sentence beginning with 'فمنهم من يقول'. This is the first of three positions. Note that the muqallid is called a believer (mu'min) but also described as disobedient (āṣī) due to abandoning the knowledge that comes from correct reasoning (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ).
The term 'ma'rifa' here refers to the knowledge of Allah that should be attained through reasoning.
3
Understand the reasoning
Ask yourself: why is the muqallid considered a believer? Because he affirms the basic tenets of faith, even without independent reasoning. Why is he sinful? Because he fails to fulfill the religious obligation to seek certainty through personal reflection.
This position distinguishes between having valid faith and fulfilling the duty of 'naẓar' (reasoning).
4
Reflect on the gloss
The gloss (1) clarifies that the prohibition of taqlid refers to its permissibility despite the validity of the imitator's faith. That is, taqlid is not allowed, but if one does imitate, his faith is still considered sound.
This nuance avoids declaring the muqallid a disbeliever while maintaining the importance of reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم إيمان المقلد، وبيان الخلاف الحاصل فيه
ومنهم من نقل عن الجمهور ومَنْ ذُكر : عدم جواز التقليد في العقائد الدينيَّة (١) ، وأنهم اختلفوا:
- فمنهم من يقول : المقلد مؤمن إلّا أنّه عاص بترك المعرفة التي ينتجها النظر الصحيح.
- ومنهم مَن فصَّلَ فقال : هو مؤمنٌ عاص إنْ كان فيه أهليَّةٌ لفهم الصَّحيح ، وغيرُ عاص إن لم يكن فيه أهلية ذلك (٢).
ومنهم مَنْ نقل عن طائفة(۳): أَنَّ مَن قلَّدَ القرآن
النظر
(۱) قوله (عدم جواز التقليد أي مع صحة إيمانه ، وهذا هو المعول عليه. الشنواني. (۲) قوله: (ومنهم من فصل هذا هو الراجح وصححه السنوسي في شرح صغرى الصغرى ، قال اليوسي: قلت وهو الظاهر، وحكاه السنوسي أيضا في شرح الحوضية ولم يعترضه، وحكاه أيضا في شرح الجزائرية، وعلله بقوله : لأن إيجاب النظر على ما لا قابلية فيه لفهم النظر الصحيح من باب تكليفه بما لا يطاق، وقد رفعه الله عن هذه الأمة : وَلَا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا [البقرة: ٢٨٦] ورده السنوسي في شرح الوسطى فقال: إن عدم الأهلية في غاية الندور ، أو هو ليس بموجود أصلا، فإن الظاهر أن كل من معه أصل عقل التكليف فهو متمكن من المعرفة والنّظر ، وغايته صعوبة النّظر على بعض الناس دون بعض ، والعُسر ليس بمانع من التكليف بالفروع ، فكيف بأصل الإيمان ، ثم على تقدير تسليم وجوده وأن تكليفه بالنظر تكليف بما لا يطاق، فلا نسلم أن التكليف بما لا يطاق غير واقع في أصول الدين... إلى آخر كلامه والراجح الكلام الأول كما تقدم. الشنواني . (۳) قوله: (ومنهم من نقل عن طائفة) وهم الحشوية معطوف على قوله: (فمنهم الأشعري)، وليس مدخولا لقوله: (وأنهم اختلفوا) كما يدل عليه بعض عباراتهم، وكذا مأمون الخطأ كالقرآن ، فإنه لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه، وفي معناه الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فإنه مأمون الخطأ للعصمة الواجبة له ، فهذا يصح تقليده ؛ إذ الاستناد إليه يوجب من الوصول إلى الحق القطعي ما يوجب البرهان القطعي، وبين أن يكون غير مأمون الخطأ كأحد العلماء، فهذا لا الاستناد إليه في العقائد ، بل لا بد من النظر الصحيح ، لعدم الأمن على عقائده التي= يصح
The judgment of the faith of the imitator, and an explanation of the disagreement concerning it. And among them is one who transmitted from the majority (al-Jumhūr) and those mentioned: that taqlīd in religious beliefs is not permissible, and that they differed. Among them is one who says: the imitator is a believer, except that he is disobedient by leaving the knowledge that results from correct reasoning (al-naẓar al-ṣaḥīḥ). And among them is one who detailed and said: he is a believer, disobedient if he possesses the capacity (ahliyya) to understand what is correct, and not disobedient if he does not possess that capacity. And among them is one who transmitted from a group [that] whoever imitates the Qur'an... (1) His saying: 'non-permissibility of taqlīd' means with the validity of his faith, and this is what is relied upon. Al-Shanwānī. (2) His saying: 'Among them is one who detailed'—this is the preponderant view, and al-Sanūsī affirmed it in his commentary on Ṣughrā al-Ṣughrā. Al-Yūsī said: I say, it is the apparent [view], and al-Sanūsī also reported it in his commentary on al-Ḥawḍiyya and did not object to it; and he also reported it in his commentary on al-Jazā'iriyya, and he justified it by saying: because obligating reasoning (al-naẓar) upon one who has no capacity to understand correct reasoning is a kind of obligating what is unbearable, and God has removed this from this nation: 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity' [al-Baqara 2:286]. And al-Sanūsī refuted this in his commentary on al-Wusṭā, saying: lack of capacity is extremely rare, or it does not exist at all, for it is apparent that everyone who possesses the basic intellect of obligation (aṣl ʿaql al-taklīf) is able to attain knowledge and reasoning; at most, reasoning is difficult for some people but not others, and difficulty does not prevent obligation in the branches (al-furūʿ), so how about the foundation of faith? Then, assuming its existence and that obligating him to reason obligates what is unbearable, we do not accept that obligating what is unbearable does not occur in the fundamentals of religion... to the end of his speech, and the preponderant is the first speech as previously mentioned. Al-Shanwānī. (3) His saying: 'And among them is one who transmitted from a group'—they are the Ḥashwiyya, and it is conjoined to his saying: 'among them is al-Ashʿarī', and it is not governed by his saying: 'and that they differed' as indicated by some of their expressions. Likewise, one who is immune from error like the Qur'an—falsehood cannot come to it from before it or behind it—and similarly the Messenger, may God bless him and grant him peace, for he is immune from error due to the compulsory infallibility (al-ʿiṣma), so his taqlīd is valid, because reliance on him necessitates access to certain truth that necessitates certain proof. And as for one who is not immune from error like any scholar, then one does not rely on him in beliefs; rather, correct reasoning is necessary, due to lack of security for his beliefs which... [text cut off]
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the theological ruling on taqlīd (imitation) in matters of faith, specifically the faith of the muqallid (one who imitates without independent reasoning). It begins by stating the title and then presents three positions. The first position, attributed to the majority, is that taqlīd in religious beliefs is not permissible, yet the imitator may still be a believer but is sinful for abandoning the knowledge that correct reasoning should produce. The second position makes a distinction: if the imitator has the capacity to understand correct reasoning, he is a believer and sinful; if he lacks that capacity, he is not sinful because God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity. This is supported by a long gloss from al-Shanwānī citing al-Sanūsī, al-Yūsī, and the verse from al-Baqara, along with a counter-argument from al-Sanūsī that lack of capacity is extremely rare. The third position, transmitted from a group (the Ḥashwiyya), concerns imitation of an infallible source like the Qur'an or the Prophet, which is valid because it leads to certain truth. In contrast, imitating a fallible scholar is not sufficient; one must engage in correct reasoning. The passage ends abruptly with a note about lack of security for one's beliefs.

Key Terms
taqlīd
muqallid
naẓar
maʿrifa

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if taqlid is not permissible, the imitator is automatically a disbeliever.
Correction: The first position clarifies that the muqallid remains a believer, though he is sinful for neglecting the duty of reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the first position, what is the status of the muqallid?
A) He is a disbeliever because he did not reason.
B) He is a believer but sinful.
C) He is not sinful if he lacks capacity.
D) His faith is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The first position affirms the faith of the imitator while condemning his neglect of personal reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you satisfied to hold beliefs simply because you were raised with them, or have you personally investigated and reached certainty? How does this lesson challenge your current approach?
?
The muqallid is called sinful for neglecting reasoning. Do you feel a responsibility to deepen your own understanding of the fundamentals of faith? What is one concrete step you will take this week?
?
If you already consider yourself a believer, does knowing that imitation is sinful make you uncomfortable? How will you respond to this warning in your daily life?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

The Second Position: Capacity-Based Judgment
Detailed position on the muqallid based on capacity, followed by a scholarly debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second position on the muqallid: that his sinfulness depends on whether he has the capacity to understand correct reasoning.

Core Idea
Sinfulness depends on capacity; those unable to reason are not culpable.

The second position introduces a crucial nuance: not all imitators are equally sinful. If a person lacks the intellectual capacity to engage in proper reasoning about beliefs, he cannot be blamed for not doing so. This lesson examines this view and the debate it sparked among scholars, especially concerning the verse 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start with the Arabic text and English translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if he has capacity... if he does not have capacity'.
The passage includes a long gloss (2) that summarizes the debate between al-Sanūsī and others.
2
Identify the detailed position
The sentence beginning with 'ومنهم مَن فصَّلَ' presents the second view. It distinguishes between a muqallid who has the capacity (ahliyya) to understand correct reasoning and one who does not.
The term 'aṣl ʿaql al-taklīf' appears in the gloss; it means the basic intellect that makes one accountable.
3
Understand the capacity argument
The key justification is from the Quran: 'God does not burden a soul beyond its capacity'. If a person cannot reason properly, requiring him to do so would be an unbearable burden.
Al-Sanūsī countered that lack of capacity is extremely rare; most people can reason to some degree.
4
Reflect on the debate
The gloss shows that al-Sanūsī initially affirmed this position in some works but later refuted it in al-Wusṭā, arguing that inability is rare and difficulty does not remove obligation.
This debate highlights the tension between mercy and the duty to seek certain knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

- فمنهم من يقول : المقلد مؤمن إلّا أنّه عاص بترك المعرفة التي ينتجها النظر الصحيح.
- ومنهم مَن فصَّلَ فقال : هو مؤمنٌ عاص إنْ كان فيه أهليَّةٌ لفهم الصَّحيح ، وغيرُ عاص إن لم يكن فيه أهلية ذلك (٢).
ومنهم مَنْ نقل عن طائفة(۳): أَنَّ مَن قلَّدَ القرآن
النظر

?

?

?

Faith and Disbelief

The Third Position: Valid Taqlid from Infallible Sources
Third position distinguishing valid taqlid from infallible sources vs. fallible scholars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third position regarding taqlid: that imitating an infallible source (the Qur'an or Prophet) is valid, while imitating fallible scholars is not sufficient for belief.

Core Idea
Taqlid is only valid when the source is infallible and leads to certain truth.

The third position introduces a key distinction: not all taqlid is the same. Imitating the Qur'an or the Prophet, who are immune from error, yields certainty and is therefore permissible. In contrast, imitating a fallible scholar does not provide security for one's beliefs; proper reasoning must be undertaken. This lesson explores this view and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin with the Arabic and English. Notice it starts with 'And among them is one who transmitted from a group'. The text then describes the types of sources that can be imitated.
The passage ends abruptly; it is cut off mid-sentence.
2
Identify the third position
The phrase 'ومنهم مَنْ نقل عن طائفة' introduces the third view. The group is identified in the gloss as the Ḥashwiyya, who held that taqlid of infallible sources is valid.
The gloss explains that this is conjoined to a previous mention of al-Ashʿarī, not part of the disagreement.
3
Understand the infallibility condition
The passage states that one who is immune from error (ma'mūn al-khaṭa') like the Qur'an or the Prophet can be imitated because their taqlid leads to certain truth. The Qur'an's immunity is proven by the verse: 'Falsehood cannot come to it from before it or behind it'.
The Prophet's immunity is based on the doctrine of ʿiṣma (infallibility).
4
Contrast with fallible scholars
The final part contrasts with scholars who are not immune from error. In their case, taqlid is not valid; one must engage in correct reasoning because there is no guarantee that their beliefs are free from error.
The sentence ends with 'due to lack of security for his beliefs which...' (cut off).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'You alone we worship and You alone we seek help' daily, are you truly relying on the Qur'an alone for your understanding of worship, or have you unconsciously adopted beliefs from popular speakers without verifying them against the infallible source? What will you do today to ensure your core beliefs are derived directly from the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
Consider a belief you hold about Allah's attributes. Did you arrive at it through your own reasoning (naẓar) or by trusting a respected teacher? This lesson warns that taqlid from fallible sources does not guarantee certainty. How can you test whether your belief is truly certain and not merely inherited?
?
If you were asked to abandon every belief that you only accepted because a scholar said it—keeping only what you know from the infallible sources—how much of your creed would remain intact? Are you ready to rebuild your faith on only that which is immune from error?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate

Majority View on the Muqallid's Faith and Sinfulness
Sanusi reports the majority view and the differentiated opinion on taqlid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two major opinions within the majority regarding the faith and sinfulness of a muqallid (one who follows blindly in creed).

Core Idea
A muqallid may be a believer, but sinfulness depends on capacity to reason.

This passage from Sanusi presents the central debate about taqlid in creedal matters. While the majority holds that taqlid is impermissible for beliefs, they disagree on whether the muqallid is sinful. Sanusi reports two sub-opinions: one holds that all muqallids are sinful for abandoning reasoning, while the other differentiates based on the person's capacity to engage in sound reasoning. Let's examine these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without focusing on the footnotes. Then read the translation. Note that the passage reports two distinct opinions.
The footnotes contain commentary from later scholars, but focus on the core text first.
2
Identify the two sub-opinions
Find where the passage says 'فمنهم من يقول' and 'ومنهم من فصل'. These introduce the two sub-opinions. Note the key difference: the first does not mention capacity, the second does.
The second opinion uses the term 'أهلية' (capacity).
3
Understand the role of capacity
Ask yourself: why does the second opinion condition sinfulness on capacity? How does this connect to the concept of 'taklif ma la yutaq' (obligating the unbearable)? The commentary elaborates on this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم إيمان المقلد، وبيان الخلاف الحاصل فيه
ومنهم من نقل عن الجمهور ومَنْ ذُكر : عدم جواز التقليد في العقائد الدينيَّة (١) ، وأنهم اختلفوا:
- فمنهم من يقول : المقلد مؤمن إلّا أنّه عاص بترك المعرفة التي ينتجها النظر الصحيح.
- ومنهم مَن فصَّلَ فقال : هو مؤمنٌ عاص إنْ كان فيه أهليَّةٌ لفهم الصَّحيح ، وغيرُ عاص إن لم يكن فيه أهلية ذلك (٢).
(١) قوله (عدم جواز التقليد أي مع صحة إيمانه ، وهذا هو المعول عليه. الشنواني.
(٢) قوله: (ومنهم من فصل هذا هو الراجح وصححه السنوسي في شرح صغرى الصغرى ، قال اليوسي: قلت وهو الظاهر، وحكاه السنوسي أيضا في شرح الحوضية ولم يعترضه، وحكاه أيضا في شرح الجزائرية، وعلله بقوله : لأن إيجاب النظر على ما لا قابلية فيه لفهم النظر الصحيح من باب تكليفه بما لا يطاق، وقد رفعه الله عن هذه الأمة : وَلَا يُكَلِّفُ اللَّهُ نَفْسًا إِلَّا وُسْعَهَا [البقرة: ٢٨٦] ورده السنوسي في شرح الوسطى فقال: إن عدم الأهلية في غاية الندور ، أو هو ليس بموجود أصلا، فإن الظاهر أن كل من معه أصل عقل التكليف فهو متمكن من المعرفة والنّظر ، وغايته صعوبة النّظر على بعض الناس دون بعض ، والعُسر ليس بمانع من التكليف بالفروع ، فكيف بأصل الإيمان ، ثم على تقدير تسليم وجوده وأن تكليفه بالنظر تكليف بما لا يطاق، فلا نسلم أن التكليف بما لا يطاق غير واقع في أصول الدين... إلى آخر كلامه والراجح الكلام الأول كما تقدم. الشنواني .
The ruling on the faith of the one who follows blindly, and an explanation of the disagreement that has occurred concerning it. And among them is one who reported from the majority and those mentioned: the impermissibility of taqlid in religious beliefs (1), and that they differed: - Among them are those who say: the muqallid is a believer, except that he is disobedient for abandoning the knowledge that sound reasoning produces. - And among them are those who detailed and said: he is a believer and disobedient if he has the capacity to understand what is sound, and not disobedient if he does not have the capacity for that (2). (1) His saying: the impermissibility of taqlid, i.e., along with the soundness of his faith, and this is what is relied upon. Al-Shanawani. (2) His saying: (and among them are those who detailed) this is the preferred view, and al-Sanusi confirmed it in his commentary on Sughra al-Sughra. Al-Yusi said: I say it is apparent. Al-Sanusi also mentioned it in his commentary on al-Hawdiyya and did not object to it, and also in his commentary on al-Jaza'iriyya, and he justified it by saying: indeed, requiring reasoning of one who has no capacity to understand sound reasoning is a kind of obligating what is unbearable, and Allah has lifted that from this nation: 'Allah does not burden a soul except with its capacity' [Quran 2:286]. But al-Sanusi refuted this in his commentary on al-Wusta, saying: lack of capacity is extremely rare, or it does not exist at all. For it is apparent that everyone who has the basic intellect of moral obligation is capable of knowledge and reasoning, and the limit is the difficulty of reasoning for some people but not others, and difficulty does not prevent obligation in subsidiary matters, so how about the foundation of faith? Then, assuming its existence and that obligating him with reasoning is obligating what is unbearable, we do not concede that obligating what is unbearable is impossible in the fundamentals of religion... to the end of his words, and the preferred view is the first one as previously mentioned. Al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the disagreement about the faith of the muqallid (one who follows religious beliefs without personal reasoning). The author first reports the majority view: taqlid in creedal matters is impermissible. Within this view, there are two sub-opinions. The first says the muqallid is a believer but sinful for not performing the obligatory reasoning (al-nazar) that leads to knowledge. The second opinion differentiates: if the person has the capacity (ahliyya) to engage in sound reasoning, then he is sinful; but if he lacks that capacity, he is not sinful. The commentator (al-Shanawani) then explains each point. For the first sub-opinion, he clarifies that 'impermissibility of taqlid' means the faith is still valid, but the act of not reasoning is sinful. For the second sub-opinion, he reports that al-Sanusi considered it the preferred view, citing an argument based on impossibility of unbearable obligation. Al-Sanusi initially supports it with Quran 2:286, but later refutes it in another work, arguing that lack of capacity is extremely rare. He states that every morally responsible person generally has the capacity for reasoning; difficulty does not excuse. The passage concludes that the first view (that the muqallid is sinful) remains the stronger one according to al-Shanawani.

Key Terms
مقلد (muqallid)
تقليد (taqlid)
النظر (al-nazar)
عاص ('asi)
أهلية (ahliyya)
تكليف ما لا يطاق (taklif ma la yutaq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Taqlid invalidates faith completely.
Correction: The majority holds that taqlid is impermissible but the muqallid's faith remains valid; he may be sinful for not reasoning, but his faith is not nullified.

Checkpoint
According to the first sub-opinion in this passage, what is the ruling on the muqallid's faith and his sinfulness?
A) His faith is invalid and he is sinful.
B) His faith is valid but he is sinful for abandoning reasoning.
C) His faith is valid and he is not sinful if he has no capacity.
D) His faith is invalid even if he cannot reason.

Key Takeaway
The muqallid's faith is valid, but he may be sinful for failing to perform reasoning, with sinfulness contingent on his capacity to reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm your faith, do you rely on what you were taught without personally seeking evidence? If so, does this lesson make you fear that you might be disobedient?
?
Consider your own capacity to reason: have you ever tried to learn the rational proofs for God's existence or other core beliefs? If not, what is holding you back from seeking the knowledge that sound reasoning produces?
?
If you knew that neglecting reasoned reflection on creed could be a sin depending on your capacity, would you change your approach to learning your faith today? What will you do about it?

Faith and Disbelief

Valid Taqlid: The Quran and Definite Sunnah
Sanusi reports the view that taqlid of infallible sources is valid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that taqlid of the Quran and definitive Sunnah is valid because they are infallible, while taqlid of fallible sources in creed is not.

Core Idea
Following infallible sources secures certain faith; following fallible ones does not.

This passage presents a view that distinguishes between acceptable and unacceptable taqlid in matters of faith. The author reports a group that holds taqlid of the Quran and definitive Sunnah is valid because they are free from error, but taqlid of others is not. This clarifies an important nuance: not all taqlid is blameworthy in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the main ruling and the reasoning behind it.
Notice the distinction between 'definitive' (qat'iyya) sources and others.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two cases: taqlid of Quran/Sunnah (valid) and taqlid of other sources (invalid). Note why each is judged as such.
3
Understand infallibility
The commentary explains why Quran and Prophet are infallible. Understand the terms 'ma'mun al-khata' and 'ma'sum'.
The Quran is safeguarded from error; the Prophet is infallible by necessity.
4
Reflect on the role of certainty
Consider why certainty (qat') is central: following infallible sources leads to definitive truth, while fallible sources do not guarantee certainty in creed.
This connects to the broader kalam principle that matters of faith require certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم مَنْ نقل عن طائفة(٣): أَنَّ مَن قلَّدَ القرآن والسنَّة القطعية صح إيمانُه ; لاتِّباعه القطعي ، ومَن قلّد غير ذلك لم يصحّ إيمانُه ; لعدم أمن الخطأ على غير المعصوم.
(٣) قوله: (ومنهم من نقل عن طائفة) وهم الحشوية معطوف على قوله: (فمنهم الأشعري)، وليس مدخولا لقوله: (وأنهم اختلفوا) كما يدل عليه بعض عباراتهم، وكذا مأمون الخطأ كالقرآن ، فإنه لا يأتيه الباطل من بين يديه ولا من خلفه، وفي معناه الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فإنه مأمون الخطأ للعصمة الواجبة له ، فهذا يصح تقليده ; إذ الاستناد إليه يوجب من الوصول إلى الحق القطعي ما يوجب البرهان القطعي، وبين أن يكون غير مأمون الخطأ كأحد العلماء، فهذا لا الاستناد إليه في العقائد ، بل لا بد من النظر الصحيح ، لعدم الأمن على عقائده التي يصح
(1) أي قطعية الثبوت من حيث المتن ، وقطعية الدلالة، لأن التقليد في المدلولات ، فعلى ذلك لا يتأتى التقليد إلا فيما كانت دلالته قطعية من كتاب أو سنة. تأمل. والله أعلم.
(2) قوله: (ومن قلد غير ذلك بأن قلد آحاد العلماء، والسنة التي ليست متواترة وإن كانت صحيحة . الشنواني.
And among them is one who reported from a group (3): that whoever follows the Quran and the definitive Sunnah, his faith is valid, due to his following what is definitive; and whoever follows other than that, his faith is not valid, due to the lack of security from error in the non-infallible. (3) His saying: (and among them is one who reported from a group) they are the Hashwiyya, it is conjoined to his saying: (so among them is al-Ash'ari), and it is not included in his saying: (and that they differed) as indicated by some of their expressions. Likewise, the one safeguarded from error, like the Quran, for falsehood does not come to it from before it or behind it, and in its meaning is the Messenger, peace be upon him, for he is safeguarded from error due to the necessary infallibility. So following such a one is valid, because relying on it leads to reaching definitive truth that definitive proof requires. And [as for] the one who is not safeguarded from error, like any of the scholars, one cannot rely on him in matters of belief; rather, sound reasoning is necessary, due to the lack of security for one's beliefs that are sound? (1) i.e., definitive in transmission (thubut) as to the text, and definitive in meaning (dalala), because taqlid pertains to meanings; accordingly, taqlid is only possible in what has definitive indication from the Book or Sunnah. Reflect. And Allah knows best. (2) His saying: (and whoever follows other than that) i.e., he follows individual scholars, and the Sunnah that is not mutawatir even if it is sahih. Al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the view of a group (identified as the Hashwiyya) regarding taqlid. The main text states that if a person follows (yuqallid) the Quran and the definitive Sunnah (al-sunnah al-qat'iyya), his faith is valid because he is following something definitive. But if he follows anything else, like scholars, his faith is not valid because non-infallible sources can err. The commentator explains that this group is separate from the previous groups. He notes that the Quran and the Prophet are infallible and free from error, so following them is not problematic; it leads to certain truth. In contrast, scholars are not infallible, so one cannot rely on them in creed without proper reasoning. The footnotes further define 'definitive' as both definite transmission and definite indication, and clarify that taqlid in meanings is only possible when the source has definitive indication. The second footnote clarifies that 'other than that' includes individual scholars and non-mutawatir hadith. Thus, the passage distinguishes between acceptable following of infallible sources and unacceptable taqlid of fallible humans.

Key Terms
طائفة (ta'ifa)
حشوية (Hashwiyya)
قطعية (qat'iyya)
معصوم (ma'sum)
مأمون الخطأ (ma'mun al-khata')
برهان قطعي (burhan qat'i)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think taqlid is always invalid in matters of faith.
Correction: This passage shows that taqlid of infallible sources (Quran, Prophet) is valid and leads to certain faith.

Checkpoint
Why according to this passage is taqlid of the Quran and definitive Sunnah valid?
A) Because they are transmitted by many narrators.
B) Because they are infallible and free from error, leading to certain truth.
C) Because they are easier to understand.
D) Because the muqallid has no capacity to reason.

Key Takeaway
Taqlid of infallible sources (Quran and definitive Sunnah) is valid and yields certain faith, unlike taqlid of fallible scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your belief in Allah, do you rely more on what you have heard from people or on what Allah and His Messenger have clearly stated? Which of these truly gives you certainty?
?
You follow a scholar or teacher in matters of creed. What would you do if you discovered they made an error in something they taught you? Is your faith built on their fallible opinion or on the infallible source itself?
?
Do you have the courage to give up a belief you were taught by a respected person if you found it contradicted the Quran and authentic Sunnah? How would you handle the discomfort of that realization?

Reasoning as a Condition of Perfection in Faith
Sanusi presents and refutes the view that reasoning is a condition of perfection, and distinguishes two types of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reasoning is a condition of perfection in faith, not a condition of its validity, and how basic reflection suffices for a valid faith.

Core Idea
Reasoning perfects faith, but simple reflection on creation is enough for valid belief.

Sanusi addresses a key question: Is reasoning necessary for faith? Some scholars hold that reasoning is only a condition of perfection, not a condition of validity. In this passage, he explains this view and refutes objections, including the notion that even simple believers like Bedouins have faith without reasoning. We will see how the Qur'an and creation themselves invite reflection, and how advanced kalam is a communal duty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main opinion
Read the first part: 'ومنهم: مَن جعل النظر والاستدلال شرط كمال فيه'. Understand that this views reasoning as a condition of perfection, not validity.
The opinion is explained further in the commentary as mustahabb.
2
Examine the refutation of blind taqlid
Notice how the commentator refutes the idea that one can blindly follow scholars. He argues that sound reasoning is needed to interpret the Quran and hadith correctly.
This part emphasizes that even understanding revelation requires reasoning.
3
Analyze the Bedouin example and two types of reasoning
See the objection from Bedouins and the refutation that they do reason through simple reflection. Then distinguish between 'tariq al-'amma' (general way) and 'tariq al-mutakallimin' (kalam way).
Advanced kalam is a communal obligation for those qualified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم: مَن جعل النظر والاستدلال شرط كمال فيه(٣)
العلماء من استند فيها إليهم من الخطأ والبدعة بل ومن الكفر الصريح لعدم وجود العصمة لآحاد الخطأ في أقوالهم وأفعالهم ، وهذا القول ضعيف جدا ; لأنه لا يعرف حقيقة القرآن والرسول ليقلدهما .. إلّا بَعْدَ النظر الصحيح المبلغ إلى معرفة الله ومعرفة رسوله، وذلك مناف للتقليد ، وأيضا ففي القرآن وأقوال الرسول من الظواهر في أصول العقائد ما يجب صرفه عن ظاهره ، والبقاء فيه على ظاهره يوجب التجسيم والتشريك وأنواعاً من البدع والكفر والعياذ بالله ، ولا تصرف هذه الأقوال عن ظاهرها إلا بالنظر الصحيح ، فبطل إذا هذا التعريف الذي سلكه هذا القائل . الشنواني .
(3) قوله: (شرط) كمال) أي : فهو مستحب ، فإيمان المقلد صحيح ولا عصيان عليه ، وإنما ترك وهو المعرفة ، احتج ذلك القائل : بأنه لو كان يكتفي في الإيمان من الأعراب ، وليسوا أهلا للنظر .. بالتَّلْفُظِ بكلمتي الشهادة المبني على الاعتقاد الجازم، ويقاس غير الإيمان عليه . ورُدَّ : بأنا لا نسلم أنّ الأعراب ليسوا أهلا للنظر ، فإن المعتبر النظر على طريق العامة ، كما أجاب به الأعرابي الأصمعي عن سؤاله: بم عرفت ربك ؟ فقال: البعرة تدل على البعير، وأثر الأقدام يدل على المسير ، فسماء ذات أبراج وأرض ذات فجاج ألا تدل على اللطيف الخبير، وما يذعن أحد من الأعراب أو غيرهم للإيمان فيأتي بكلمتيه إلا بعد أن ينظر فيهتدي لذلك. وأما النظر على طريق المتكلمين من تحرير الأدلة وتدقيقها ودفع الشكوك والشبه عنها ففرض كفاية في حق المتأهلين له يكفي قيام بعضهم به، وأما غيرهم ممن يخشى عليهم من الخوض فيه الوقوع في الشبه والضلال فليس لهم الخوض فيه . اهـ . مؤلف في كبيره الشنواني .
And among them is one who made reasoning and inference a condition of perfection in it (3). The scholars from whom he relied upon them for error and innovation, rather even explicit disbelief, due to the lack of infallibility for individuals from error in their words and actions, and this statement is very weak; because one does not know the reality of the Quran and the Messenger to follow them except after sound reasoning that leads to knowledge of Allah and knowledge of His Messenger, and that is contrary to taqlid. Also, in the Quran and the sayings of the Messenger there are apparent meanings in the fundamentals of belief that must be interpreted away from their apparent meaning, and remaining upon their apparent meaning leads to anthropomorphism, associationism, and various kinds of innovation and disbelief—Allah forbid. And these sayings are not interpreted away from their apparent meaning except by sound reasoning. Hence, this definition that this proponent followed is invalid. Al-Shanawani. (3) His saying: (condition of perfection) i.e., it is recommended (mustahabb). So the faith of the muqallid is valid, and there is no disobedience upon him; only he has left what is recommended, which is knowledge. That proponent argued that if one were satisfied, in the faith of the Bedouins who are not capable of reasoning, with the utterance of the two testimonies based on firm belief, then others are analogized to that. And it was refuted by saying: we do not concede that the Bedouins are not capable of reasoning; rather, what is considered is reasoning in the general way. As the Bedouin al-Asma'i answered when asked: 'How did you know your Lord?' He said: 'Dung indicates the camel, and footprints indicate the path; so a sky with constellations and an earth with valleys—do they not indicate the All-Subtle, the All-Aware?' And no Bedouin or anyone else submits to faith and utters the two testimonies except after he reflects and is guided thereby. As for reasoning in the way of the mutakallimun, involving formulation of proofs, their precision, and repelling doubts and ambiguities, it is a communal obligation (fard kifaya) for those qualified for it; it suffices that some of them undertake it. As for others who fear that engaging in it may lead them to fall into ambiguities and misguidance, they should not engage in it. End. Author in his larger work. Al-Shanawani.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage discusses the view that reasoning (al-nazar) and inference (al-istidlal) are a condition of perfection (shart kamal) for faith, not a condition of validity. The commentator first refutes an objection that would require taqlid of scholars, arguing that one cannot even understand the Quran and Prophet correctly without sound reasoning because apparent meanings can lead to heresy if not properly interpreted. Then the commentator explains the view itself: reasoning is mustahabb (recommended), not obligatory; the muqallid's faith is valid and he is not sinful. The proponent argued that Bedouins are accepted as believers without deep reasoning. The refutation (al-radd) states that Bedouins do have a basic form of reasoning—they reflect on creation and arrive at belief. Their testimony is based on such reflection, not blind following. The passage distinguishes between two types of reasoning: the simple reflection of the common people (طريق العامة) and the technical reasoning of the mutakallimun (طريق المتكلمين). The latter is a communal obligation for those qualified, but others should avoid it if it may lead to confusion. Thus, the passage clarifies that the required reasoning for a valid faith is the basic reflection, not the advanced theological argumentation.

Key Terms
شرط كمال
مستحب
النظر
طريق العامة
طريق المتكلمين
فرض كفاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reasoning is absolutely necessary for faith to be valid; without it, faith is invalid.
Correction: The passage teaches that reasoning is a condition of perfection, not validity. The faith of a muqallid is valid, and even Bedouins have basic reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation in this passage, why is the Bedouin's belief not considered pure taqlid without reasoning?
A) Because Bedouins are naturally intelligent.
B) Because the Bedouin al-Asma'i gave a proof from creation.
C) Because the Bedouin was a scholar.
D) Because the Bedouin had no choice.

Key Takeaway
Reasoning is a perfection in faith, but the basic reflection on creation is sufficient for valid belief; advanced kalam is a communal obligation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that reasoning is a perfection, not a requirement. But are you content with a valid faith that lacks perfection? What does it say about your love for Allah if you are satisfied with the minimum? Commit today to spend a few minutes reflecting on one sign of creation to move from valid to perfect faith.
?
The Bedouin al-Asma'i recognized his Lord from dung and footprints. When did you last look at the world around you and see clear evidence of your Creator? Is your faith so strong that you see Him in everything, or have you become blind to these signs? Pause now and find one thing in your immediate environment that points to Allah, and truly reflect on it.
?
Advanced theological reasoning is a communal obligation for some, but the passage warns that for others it may lead to confusion. Are you someone who dives into theological debates out of pride or curiosity, while neglecting your personal reflection and sincerity? Honestly ask yourself: do you seek knowledge to draw closer to Allah or to win arguments? What would change if you prioritized the first?

Faith and Disbelief

Ruling on the Faith of the Muqallid
Ruling on the muqallid's faith: two scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions under which a muqallid's faith is considered valid according to definitive texts.

Core Idea
Faith of a muqallid is only valid when following what is definitive in both transmission and meaning.

This passage addresses an important theological question: can one who merely follows authority have valid faith? The answer depends on what they follow. The text distinguishes between following definitive texts, which yields certainty, and following non-definitive sources, which risks error. Let's explore the ruling and its reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two contrasting rulings.
The passage is concise, so pay attention to the footnotes.
2
Identify the two rulings
The passage gives a ruling for two cases: following definitive Sunnah and following other than that. Identify what makes each valid or invalid.
3
Understand the key terms
The commentary explains 'definitive' means certainty in both transmission and meaning. Reflect on why this matters for faith.
This connects to the requirement of certainty in matters of faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم إيمان المقلد، وبيان الخلاف الحاصل فيه
والسُّنَّة القطعية (۱) صح إيمانُه ؛ لاتِّباعه القطعي ، ومَن قلّد غير ذلك (۲) لم يصحّ إيمانُه ؛ لعدم أمن الخطأ على غير المعصوم.
(۱) أي قطعية الثبوت من حيث المتن ، وقطعية الدلالة، لأن التقليد في المدلولات ، فعلى ذلك لا يتأتى التقليد إلا فيما كانت دلالته قطعية من كتاب أو سنة. تأمل. والله أعلم.
(۲) قوله: (ومن قلد غير ذلك بأن قلد آحاد العلماء، والسنة التي ليست متواترة وإن كانت صحيحة . الشنواني .
The ruling on the faith of the muqallid (blind follower), and an explanation of the disagreement concerning it.
And the definitive Sunnah (al-sunnah al-qaṭʿiyyah): (1) his faith is valid, because of his following of that which is definitive; and whoever follows other than that (2), his faith is not valid, because of the lack of safety from error regarding one who is not infallible.
(1) Meaning: definitive in its textual transmission (thubūt) in terms of the text (matn), and definitive in its indication (dalālah), because taqlīd (following) applies to the indicated meanings; accordingly, taqlīd is only possible in matters whose indication is definitive from the Book or Sunnah. Reflect. And God knows best.
(2) His statement: 'and whoever follows other than that'—i.e. whoever follows individual scholars, and the Sunnah which is not mutawātir (massively transmitted) even if it is ṣaḥīḥ (authentic). Al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and classification. It opens by stating the ruling on the faith of the muqallid (one who follows authority without independent reasoning). The text first presents the view that the faith of a muqallid who follows what is definitively established (al-sunnah al-qaṭʿiyyah) is valid, because his following is based on certainty. Then it presents the opposite: if he follows something that is not definitive, his faith is not valid, because non-infallible sources are prone to error. The commentary in the footnotes explains the key terms: 'definitive' here means both the text's transmission and its meaning are certain; taqlīd only applies when the indication is definitive from the Qur'an or Sunnah. The second footnote specifies that following other scholars or non-mutawātir Sunnah does not constitute valid taqlīd in matters of faith.

Key Terms
مقلد
سنة قطعية
قطعيات الثبوت
قطعيات الدلالة
متواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that following any scholar or hadith is sufficient for valid faith.
Correction: The passage teaches that only following definitive (qaṭʿī) texts—certain in transmission and meaning—makes faith valid; following non-definitive sources does not.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the faith of a muqallid considered valid?
A) When he follows any scholar or hadith, even if not definitive.
B) When he follows the definitive Sunnah (al-sunnah al-qaṭʿiyyah) in both text and meaning.
C) When he engages in independent reasoning (nazar).
D) When he follows the consensus of scholars.

Key Takeaway
Faith based on taqlid is only valid when the source followed is definitive in both transmission and meaning, ensuring certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about Allah simply because a respected teacher or scholar told you, without verifying that it comes from a definitive, certain source? What does this lesson teach you about the risk of such blind following, and what will you do to ensure your faith is built on certainty?
?
Think of one core Islamic belief that you hold. Can you identify whether the evidence for it is definitive (qaṭʿī) or probable (ẓannī) in both its transmission and meaning? If you realize it is not definitive, what step will you take today to ground it in certain knowledge?
?
This lesson shows that not all taqlīd is valid—only taqlīd of what is certain. Does your current approach to learning ‘aqīdah rely more on trusted authorities or on verifying the certainty of the sources? Are you willing to change your learning habits so that your faith is not left vulnerable to error?

Refutation of Nazar as a Condition of Perfection
Refutes the view that nazar is a condition of perfection and distinguishes levels of reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that independent reasoning (nazar) is only a condition of perfection for faith is invalid, and how the response distinguishes between common reasoning (obligatory for all) and detailed theological reasoning (collective obligation for qualified scholars).

Core Idea
Reasoning in faith is not optional; even simple reflection is a form of nazar.

This passage from the Ithāf al-Murīd addresses an objection that independent reasoning (al-naẓar) is only commendable for faith, not required. The author refutes this by showing that even basic faith requires some reasoning, and that the apparent meanings of scripture demand correct interpretation. The footnote then clarifies the distinction between common reasoning and the detailed reasoning of theologians.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation together. Notice how the author first states the view to be refuted, then presents two arguments against it.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two refutation arguments
The first argument: without correct reasoning, one cannot know the Qur'an and the Prophet to follow them. The second argument: the apparent meanings of scripture in creedal matters must be interpreted, and only reasoning can do that.
These show that reasoning is indispensable, not merely commendable.
3
Examine the objector's case and the response
The footnote presents the objector's claim based on bedouins. Then the response shows that even bedouins engage in simple reasoning (like the story of al-Aṣmaʿī).
4
Understand the two types of reasoning
Common reasoning (naẓar ʿāmm) is obligatory for all. Theological reasoning (ṭarīq al-mutakallimīn) is a collective obligation (farḍ kifāyah) for those qualified, and even forbidden for those who might fall into doubt.
This resolves the apparent tension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم: مَن جعل النظر والاستدلال شرط كمال فيه(۳)
العلماء من استند فيها إليهم من الخطأ والبدعة بل ومن الكفر الصريح لعدم وجود العصمة لآحاد الخطأ في أقوالهم وأفعالهم ، وهذا القول ضعيف جدا ؛ لأنه لا يعرف حقيقة القرآن والرسول ليقلدهما .. إلّا بَعْدَ النظر الصحيح المبلغ إلى معرفة الله ومعرفة رسوله، وذلك مناف للتقليد ، وأيضا ففي القرآن وأقوال الرسول من الظواهر في أصول العقائد ما يجب صرفه عن ظاهره ، والبقاء فيه على ظاهره يوجب التجسيم والتشريك وأنواعاً من البدع والكفر والعياذ بالله ، ولا تصرف هذه الأقوال عن ظاهرها إلا بالنظر الصحيح ، فبطل إذا هذا التعريف الذي سلكه هذا القائل . الشنواني .
(۳) قوله: (شرط) کمال) أي : فهو مستحب ، فإيمان المقلد صحيح ولا عصيان عليه ، وإنما ترك ، وهو المعرفة ، احتج ذلك القائل : بأنه لو كان يكتفي في الإيمان من الأعراب ، وليسوا أهلا للنظر .. بالتَّلْفُظِ بكلمتي الشهادة المبني على الاعتقاد الجازم، ويقاس غير مندوباً، الإيمان عليه .
ورُدَّ : بأنا لا نسلم أنّ الأعراب ليسوا أهلا للنظر ، فإن المعتبر النظر على طريق العامة ، كما أجاب به الأعرابي الأصمعي عن سؤاله: بم عرفت ربك ؟ فقال: البعرة تدل على البعير، وأثر الأقدام يدل على المسير ، فسماء ذات أبراج وأرض ذات فجاج ألا تدل على اللطيف الخبير، وما يذعن أحد من الأعراب أو غيرهم للإيمان فيأتي بكلمتيه إلا بعد أن ينظر فيهتدي لذلك. وأما النظر على طريق المتكلمين من تحرير الأدلة وتدقيقها ودفع الشكوك والشبه عنها ففرض كفاية في حق المتأهلين له يكفي قيام بعضهم به، وأما غيرهم ممن يخشى عليهم من الخوض فيه الوقوع في الشبه والضلال فليس لهم الخوض فيه . اهـ . مؤلف في كبيره الشنواني .
And among them is one who made independent reasoning (al-naẓar) and inference (al-istidlāl) a condition of perfection (sharṭ kamāl) in it (i.e., in faith). (3)
The scholars who rely on them [i.e., the authorities] [are protected] from error, innovation, and even outright disbelief because there is no infallibility for individuals from error in their statements and actions. And this statement is very weak, because one does not know the reality of the Qur'an and the Messenger to follow them except after correct reasoning that leads to knowledge of God and knowledge of His Messenger, and that is contrary to taqlīd. Also, in the Qur'an and the statements of the Messenger there are apparent meanings (ẓawāhir) in the fundamentals of creed that must be interpreted away from their apparent sense, and remaining with their apparent sense leads to anthropomorphism (tajsīm), associationism (tashrīk), and types of innovation and disbelief—God forbid! And these statements cannot be diverted from their apparent sense except by correct reasoning. Thus this definition that this proponent followed is invalid. Al-Shanwānī.
(3) His statement: 'a condition of perfection'—meaning it is commendable (mustaḥabb), so the faith of the muqallid is valid and there is no disobedience upon him, but he merely leaves what is better, which is knowledge (al-maʿrifah). That proponent argued that if [the Lawgiver] were satisfied in faith with bedouins, who are not people of reasoning, [accepting] the utterance of the two testimonies based on firm belief, then others would be measured against it, and [he claimed] faith is commendable there.
And it was responded: We do not concede that bedouins are not people of reasoning; rather, the consideration is reasoning in the common way (ṭarīq al-ʿāmmah), as the bedouin al-Aṣmaʿī answered when asked, 'How did you know your Lord?' He said: 'Dung indicates the camel, and the footprint indicates the journey; a sky with constellations and an earth with valleys—do they not indicate the Subtle, the Aware?' And no one, whether bedouin or others, submits to faith and utters the two testimonies except after reasoning and being guided thereby. As for reasoning in the manner of the theologians (ṭarīq al-mutakallimīn)—formulating proofs, scrutinizing them, and repelling doubts and ambiguities—it is a collective obligation (farḍ kifāyah) upon those who are qualified for it; it suffices that some of them undertake it. As for others, who fear that delving into it might cause them to fall into doubts and misguidance, they should not delve into it. End. The author in his larger [work] al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the opinion that independent reasoning (naẓar) is a condition of perfection for faith, meaning it is commendable but not obligatory. The refutation begins by noting that without correct reasoning, one cannot truly know the Qur'an and the Messenger to follow them—even for basics. Furthermore, the apparent meanings of scripture in creedal matters must be interpreted correctly, and only reasoning can accomplish that; thus the definition that makes reasoning optional is invalid. The footnote then details the argument: the proponent claimed that if faith were accepted from bedouins without reasoning, then reasoning cannot be obligatory. The response rejects this, arguing that even bedouins engage in simple reasoning (like the story of al-Aṣmaʿī). The distinction is made between common reasoning (naẓar ʿāmm) obligatory on everyone, and the advanced reasoning of theologians (mutakallimūn), which is a collective obligation (farḍ kifāyah) for those qualified, and even forbidden for those who might fall into doubt.

Key Terms
النظر
الاستدلال
شرط كمال
المقلد
فرض كفاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that reasoning in faith is either optional (only commendable) or forbidden for all.
Correction: Common reasoning (simple reflection) is obligatory for every Muslim; advanced theological reasoning is a collective duty for qualified scholars, and not to be undertaken by those who might fall into doubt.

Checkpoint
According to the response in this passage, what type of reasoning is considered a collective obligation (farḍ kifāyah)?
A) The common reasoning of bedouins.
B) The detailed reasoning of theologians (ṭarīq al-mutakallimīn).
C) The reasoning required to utter the two testimonies.
D) The reasoning that follows from observing natural signs.

Key Takeaway
Faith without any reasoning is impossible; simple reflection is obligatory for all, while advanced theological reasoning is a collective duty for qualified scholars.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the sky or the earth, do you genuinely infer the Creator as the bedouin did, or have your eyes become dull to the signs? If you are honest, what would change if you truly reflected on one creation today? Will you do it before you sleep?
?
Have you ever delegated all religious reasoning to scholars, feeling that your own simple reflection is insufficient? But the lesson says even basic reasoning is obligatory for you. What evidence from your own life—like the signs of creation—have you ignored? How can you start practicing simple reflection daily?
?
The lesson warns that remaining on the apparent meaning of scripture leads to error. Is there a belief you hold about God (like His attributes) that you have not properly reasoned through, perhaps because you fear doubt? Will you commit to seeking correct understanding from a qualified teacher, not just accepting what you grew up with?

The View Forbidding Nazar and Its Proper Interpretation
Presents the view forbidding nazar, its problematic literal meaning, and the corrective interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the literal interpretation of forbidding nazar is problematic and how scholars reconciled it with the need for reflection.

Core Idea
Forbidding nazar does not mean abandoning all reasoning—only unsuitable scrutiny.

This passage presents the third major opinion on nazar: that it is forbidden. Taken literally, this contradicts the Qur'an's own call to reflection. Scholars therefore interpret it as a prohibition against engaging in overly subtle theological doubts, especially for beginners. The rebuttal clarifies that it is detailed reasoning, not general reflection, that leads to confusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the abrupt opening: 'And among them is one who forbade reasoning.'
2
Identify the problem with the literal interpretation
The commentator explains that taking the statement literally would forbid reflecting on Qur'anic proofs, which is absurd. Find this reasoning in the text.
The problem is that the Qur'an itself contains countless proofs that require reflection.
3
Understand the interpretation and the rebuttal
The passage offers an interpretation: it forbids only engagement with subtle doubts (shubuhāt) in advanced works, especially for beginners. Then it adds a rebuttal: the cause of falling into doubts is detailed reasoning, not general reasoning.
The rebuttal (rudd) is attributed to al-Shanwānī and distinguishes between tafṣīlī and ijmālī reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم: مَن حَرَّم النَّظر (۱)
قال العلامة المحلي(۲): وقد اتفقت الطرق الثلاثة ـ يعني الموجبة للنظر
(۱) قوله: (ومنهم من حرم النظر) فإيمان المقلد صحيح ويحرم أن ينظر نظرا يؤديه للمعرفة حمل هذا الكلام على ظاهره كان مشكلا ؛ إذ يلزمه أنْ يُحرِّمَ النظر في الأدلة القرآنية ؛ إذ القرآن فيه ما لا يحصى من الأدلة، ويمكن حمله على تحريم النظر في دقائق الشبه على المبتدئ أو من هو بليد الطبع، كدقائق الشبه التي في «المقاصد» و«المواقف» خصوصا المختلط بعلوم الفلسفة . ورُدَّ : بأن الموقع في الشبه الدليل التفصيلي لا الإجمالي. الشنواني .
And among them is one who forbade reasoning (al-naẓar). (1)
The distinguished scholar al-Maḥallī (2) said: 'The three methods—meaning those that necessitate reasoning—have agreed...'
(1) His statement: 'And among them is one who forbade reasoning'— i.e., the faith of the muqallid is valid, and it is forbidden for him to reason in a way that leads to knowledge. Interpreting this statement literally is problematic, because it would necessitate forbidding reasoning on the Qur'anic proofs, for the Qur'an contains countless proofs. It can be interpreted as forbidding reasoning on the subtleties of ambiguous doubts by the beginner or one of dull nature, like the subtle doubts found in the 'Maqāṣid' and 'Mawāqif', especially when mixed with the sciences of philosophy. And it was responded: The cause of falling into doubts is detailed reasoning (al-dalīl al-tafṣīlī), not general reasoning (al-ijmālī). Al-Shanwānī.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the third opinion: some scholars hold that nazar (independent reasoning) is forbidden in matters of faith. The commentary immediately notes that taking this statement literally is problematic because it would imply that reflecting on Qur'anic proofs is forbidden, which is absurd since the Qur'an itself is full of proofs. Therefore, it is interpreted as forbidding engagement in complex, subtle doubts (shubuhāt)—the kind found in advanced theological works like the Maqāṣid and Mawāqif—especially for beginners or those of dull intellect. The rebuttal (rudd) adds that falling into doubts is caused by detailed reasoning (al-dalīl al-tafṣīlī) on specific points, not by general reasoning (al-ijmālī). Then al-Maḥallī's brief statement is cited: 'the three methods have agreed'—referring to the three opinions (obligating nazar, making it a condition of perfection, forbidding it)—implying that despite differences, they all emphasize the necessity of some form of reasoning.

Key Terms
تحريم النظر
المبتدئ
الشبه
الدليل التفصيلي
الدليل الإجمالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that forbidding nazar means all forms of reasoning are prohibited for everyone.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the prohibition applies only to engaging with complex theological subtleties (shubuhāt) and is primarily for beginners or those of dull intellect. Moreover, the root of falling into doubts is detailed reasoning, not general reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a literal interpretation of 'forbidding nazar' problematic?
A) Because it would imply that even the Prophet forbade nazar.
B) Because it would forbid reasoning on the numerous proofs in the Qur'an.
C) Because it would make faith invalid for all muqallids.
D) Because it contradicts the consensus of the companions.

Key Takeaway
The prohibition of nazar is restricted to complex theological subtleties for beginners, not to basic reflection on Qur'anic proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you blindly followed religious opinions without seeking understanding, and how does this lesson's teaching that basic reflection on the Quran is not only allowed but encouraged challenge your current practice? Will you begin reflecting on one Quranic proof today?
?
Have you ever engaged in theological debates or studies that left you more confused than certain? Could it be that you dabbled in 'subtle doubts' meant for those more advanced? Are you willing to set aside intellectual pride and return to simple reflection on clear proofs?
?
The rebuttal says that falling into doubts comes from detailed reasoning, not general. Do you tend to overanalyze matters of faith, picking apart every detail? What would it look like to content yourself with the general proofs that lead to certainty, trusting Allah's guidance without unnecessary scrutiny?

Validity of the Muqallid's Faith Across Three Opinions
Al-Mahalli states the three opinions agree on the muqallid's faith validity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the three opinions on the obligation of reflection (nazar) all agree that the faith of a muqallid (blind follower) is valid, even if he is sinful for neglecting reflection according to one view.

Core Idea
The muqallid's faith is valid but may be sinful—three opinions, one agreement.

This lesson explores a key ruling in Islamic theology: the status of a person who believes in Allah without personal reasoning (the muqallid). Scholars differed on whether reflection is obligatory, forbidden, or merely permitted, yet they all agreed on the validity of the muqallid's faith. We'll examine al-Mahalli's concise statement that captures this consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the three opinions mentioned.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify 'nazar' (reflection) and 'muqallid' (blind follower). How do the three opinions relate?
3
Analyze the agreement
See how the passage states agreement on the validity of faith, while noting the sin according to the first opinion.
The first opinion is the obligating view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم: مَن حَرَّم النَّظر. قال العلامة المحلي: وقد اتفقت الطرق الثلاثة ـ يعني الموجبة للنظر والمُحرّمة والمُجوّزة - على صحة إيمان المقلّد ، وإن كان آثما بترك النَّظر على الأول. اهـ.
And among them is one who forbade reflection. Al-Allama al-Mahalli said: The three ways—meaning the obligating, the forbidding, and the permitting—have agreed upon the soundness of the faith of the muqallid, although he is sinful for abandoning reflection according to the first [view]. End.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of the faith of the muqallid (one who follows authority without proof). The author mentions that among the scholars is one who forbids reflection altogether. Then he quotes al-Mahalli, who states that there are three opinions regarding the obligation of reflection for attaining knowledge of Allah: one that obligates it, one that forbids it, and one that permits it. Despite their disagreement on the ruling of reflection, all three opinions concur that the faith of the muqallid is valid and sound. However, according to the first opinion (that reflection is obligatory), the muqallid is sinful for abandoning reflection even though his faith is valid. This establishes that the muqallid's faith is acceptable in the sight of Allah, though he is culpable for not using his intellect to arrive at certainty.

Key Terms
إيمان المقلد
النظر
الموجبة
المحرّمة
المجوّزة
صحة الإيمان
الإثم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the muqallid is sinful, his faith is invalid.
Correction: This passage clarifies that sinfulness does not nullify the validity of faith; the muqallid's faith is valid even if he is sinful for neglecting reflection according to the obligating view.

Checkpoint
According to al-Mahalli, what do the three opinions (obligating, forbidding, permitting reflection) agree upon?
A) That reflection is obligatory
B) That the faith of the muqallid is invalid
C) That the faith of the muqallid is valid
D) That the muqallid is not sinful for abandoning reflection

Key Takeaway
The three opinions on nazar all affirm the validity of the muqallid's faith, though the obligating view deems him sinful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You now know that all three opinions agree on the validity of the muqallid's faith. Yet according to the obligating view, the muqallid is sinful. Does this make you consider whether you have ever reflected on the proofs of your own faith, or have you merely inherited it? What will you do today to engage in proper reflection?
?
If you were a muqallid, would you be content with a faith that is valid but possibly sinful (according to one view)? What step will you take to move from blind following to certainty?
?
The passage shows that sinfulness does not invalidate faith. How does this change your understanding of the gravity of neglecting reflection? Will you now take the means to acquire certainty, or remain comfortable with a faith that might carry sin?

Faith and Disbelief
beginner
5 min
Specifying What Disagreement Is Really About
The author specifies that the disagreement is about reflection leading to knowledge of messengers, not knowledge of Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the disagreement about the muqallid does not apply to reflection that leads to knowledge of Allah, but only to reflection concerning the messengers, and you will see the two key scenarios that illustrate this scope.

Core Idea
The dispute about blind faith is about believing the messengers, not about knowing Allah.

We have seen that scholars disagree about whether a muqallid can be saved. But the author now clarifies an important boundary: that disagreement does not cover the first step of faith—knowledge of Allah—which is required by consensus. Instead, it applies to the knowledge of the messengers and the details of prophethood. This lesson explains the precise scope and gives two vivid examples to illustrate the different situations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its translation. Notice that the author first makes a general statement about the scope of disagreement.
Pay attention to the terms 'محل الخلاف' and 'النظر الموصل لمعرفة الله'.
2
Identify the two parts of the passage
The passage first states what is NOT in dispute (reflection leading to knowledge of Allah) and then what IS in dispute (reflection about messengers). Then it gives two examples. Mark these two sections.
3
Consider the two examples
The first example is a person isolated on a mountain who accepts belief from a non-infallible informant without reflection. The second is a person in Islamic lands where the Prophet's miracles are widely known. Ask: Why are these different? The author is showing that the disagreement applies only to the first type.
Note the terms 'شاهق جبل' (high mountain) and 'غير معصوم' (non-infallible).
4
Reflect on the key terms
Pay special attention to 'غير معصوم' and 'التواتر'. These are crucial for understanding the cases. The first scenario lacks reliable evidence; the second has overwhelming evidence.
Tawatur means widespread transmission that gives certainty.

ومحل الخلاف في غير النظر الموصل لمعرفة الله تعالى؛ أما هو فواجب إجماعاً، كما أنَّ الخلاف إنَّما هو فيمن نشأ على شاهق جبل مثلاً ولم يتفكّر في ملكوت السَّماوات والأرض، فأخبره غير معصوم بما يُفترض عليه اعتقاده، فصدقه فيما أخبره به بمجرَّد إخباره من غير تفكر ولا تدبُّر. الخلاف فيمن نشأ في ديار الإسلام من الأمصار والقُرى والصحارى، وتواتر عندهم حال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وما أَتى به من المعجزات.
And the locus of disagreement is concerning reflection that does not lead to knowledge of Allah the Exalted; rather, it is obligatory by consensus. Also, the disagreement is only about one who grew up on a high mountain, for example, and did not reflect on the dominion of the heavens and the earth, so a non-infallible person informed him of what he is obligated to believe, and he believed him in what he informed him merely by his informing without reflection or deliberation. The disagreement is about one who grew up in the lands of Islam among the cities, villages, and deserts, and the state of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and what he brought of miracles is mutawatir among them.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage clarifies the scope of the earlier disagreement about the muqallid. It states that the disagreement is not about reflection that leads to knowledge of Allah, because that is obligatory by unanimous consensus. Rather, the disagreement concerns reflection for knowledge of the messengers or other matters. The author then gives two examples: first, a person who grew up in isolation on a mountain, never thinking about creation, and is told by a non-infallible person what he must believe, and he accepts it blindly without any reflection. In this case, there is disagreement about the ruling. Second, a person who grew up in Islamic lands where the miracles of the Prophet are widely known through tawatur. For such a person, the evidence is already present, so the case is different. The passage distinguishes between these two scenarios to show where the disagreement applies.

Misconception: The disagreement about the muqallid applies to all types of reflection, including reflection for knowledge of Allah.
Correction: It does not. Reflection for knowledge of Allah is obligatory by consensus. The disagreement is only about reflection for knowledge of messengers.
