The Faith of the Muqallid: Disagreement and Consensus
Discusses the disagreement on the muqallid's faith and the consensus of the Ashab.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the disagreement over the muqallid's faith, the consensus of the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools on its validity, and the obligation of nazar.

Core Idea
The muqallid's faith is valid by consensus, even if he is sinful for not reflecting.

This lesson examines a key debate in Islamic theology: does blind faith (taqlid) suffice for belief? The author clarifies that while nazar (rational investigation) is obligatory, the majority holds that the faith of a muqallid is valid. He distinguishes between two scenarios and reports the consensus of the Ashab (Ash'aris) and the Maturidis that the common people are true believers. Let’s explore the source text to see the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's distinction between two types of people.
The passage begins with a general ruling and then provides scenarios.
2
Identify the main positions
Identify the two positions: those who prohibit and those who permit taqlid. Note the clarification that nazar is obligatory by consensus.
The disagreement is only about the muqallid's faith, not about the obligation of nazar.
3
Understand the two scenarios
The passage describes two specific cases: a person isolated from society who accepts faith blindly, and a person raised in Islamic lands with recurrent proofs. Note that both are covered by the disagreement.
4
Note the consensus
Al-Amidi reports the consensus of the Ashab that the muqallid is not a disbeliever. Then Al-Maturidi states the unanimous view of his school that the common people are true believers. This is the key takeaway.
The only known dissenter is Abu Hashim al-Jubbai of the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم إيمان المقلد، وبيان الخلاف الحاصل فيه والمُحرّمة والمُجوّزة - على صحة إيمان المقلّد ، وإن كان آثما بترك النَّظر على الأول . اهـ . ومحل الخلاف في غير النظر الموصل لمعرفة الله تعالى ؛ أما هو فواجب إجماعاً، كما أنَّ الخلاف إنَّما هو فيمن نشأ على شاهق جبل مثلاً ولم يتفكّر في ملكوت السَّماوات والأرض، فأخبره غير معصوم بما يُفترض عليه اعتقاده ، فصدقه فيما أخبره به بمجرَّد إخباره من غير تفكر ولا تدبُّر . الخلاف فيمن نشأ في ديار الإسلام من الأمصار والقُرى والصحارى ، وتواتر عندهم حال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وما أَتى به من المعجزات ، ولا وحكى الآمدي اتفاق الأصحاب على انتفاء كفر المقلد ، وأنَّه ليس للجمهور إلا القول بعصيانه بترك النظر إن قدر عليه، مع اتفاقهم على صحة إيمانه، وأنه لا يُعرَف القولُ بعدم صحة إيمان المقلد إلا لأبي هاشم الجبائي من المعتزلة. وقال أبو منصور الماتريدي : أجمع أصحابُنَا على أنَّ العوام مؤمنونَ عارفونَ بربِّهم، وأنَّهم حَشْو الجنَّة، كما جاءت به الأخبار، وانعقد عليه
The ruling on the faith of the muqallid, and explanation of the disagreement concerning it: and the [sects that] prohibit and permit [it] – [differ] on the validity of the faith of the muqallid, even if he is sinful for abandoning nazar according to the first opinion. End. And the locus of disagreement is concerning other than the nazar that leads to knowledge of Allah the Most High; as for it, it is obligatory by consensus. Also, the disagreement is only concerning one who grew up on a towering mountain, for example, and did not reflect on the dominion of the heavens and the earth, and a non-ma‘sum informed him of what he is obliged to believe, so he believed him in what he informed him merely by his informing without reflection or deliberation. The disagreement [also concerns] one who grew up in the lands of Islam, in cities, villages, and deserts, and the condition of the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم and the miracles he brought were transmitted to him recurrently, and ... And Al-Amidi reported the consensus of the Ashab on the negation of the unbelief of the muqallid, and that the majority only hold the opinion of his disobedience for abandoning nazar if he is capable of it, along with their consensus on the validity of his faith, and that the opinion of the invalidity of the muqallid’s faith is not known except from Abu Hashim al-Jubbai of the Mu‘tazila. And Abu Mansur al-Maturidi said: Our companions are unanimous that the common people are believers, knowing their Lord, and that they are the filler of Paradise, as has come in the reports, and the consensus has been established upon it.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on the faith of the muqallid (one who follows without independent investigation) and explains the disagreement among scholars. It first notes that some sects prohibit and some permit, but the disagreement is specifically about the validity of the muqallid’s faith, with one opinion holding it valid though sinful for abandoning nazar (rational investigation). The author then clarifies that the disagreement does not concern nazar that leads to knowledge of Allah, which is obligatory by unanimous agreement. Rather, the disagreement involves two scenarios: first, a person who has never reflected and is informed by a non-ma‘sum and accepts blindly; second, a person raised in Islamic lands with ample proofs. The passage then cites Al-Amidi, who reports that the Ashab (Ash‘aris) agree the muqallid is not a disbeliever; they only say he is disobedient if capable of nazar. The only known dissenter is Abu Hashim al-Jubbai of the Mu‘tazila. Finally, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi affirms that his school unanimously considers the common believers ('awamm) to be true believers and inhabitants of Paradise.

Correction: The passage shows that the majority of scholars (Ash‘aris and Maturidis) hold that the muqallid’s faith is valid; the only dissenter is Abu Hashim al-Jubbai. They may be sinful for not performing nazar, but they remain believers.

The Faith of the Imitator (al-Muqallid): Al-Amidi's Report
Al-Amidi reports consensus on muqallid's valid faith and Abu Hashim's exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly consensus regarding the faith of the muqallid (one who imitates belief without personal reflection) and the exceptional view of Abu Hashim al-Jubbā'i.

Core Idea
The muqallid's faith is valid even if he is sinful for not reflecting.

This lesson presents a key report from Al-Amidi about the status of a muqallid—a person who holds faith without engaging in personal reasoned reflection (nazar). The report shows that the majority of scholars agreed that such a person is not a disbeliever, though he may be sinful, and that his faith is valid. The only known dissenter is Abu Hashim al-Jubbā'i from the Mu'tazila, who held the muqallid's faith invalid. Understanding this consensus helps clarify a foundational issue in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins with a general statement about reflection.
2
Identify the consensus and the exception
Find the two points of agreement: that the muqallid is not a disbeliever, and that his faith is valid. Then locate the exception: Abu Hashim al-Jubbā'i.
The phrase 'لا يُعرَف القولُ بعدم صحة إيمان المقلد إلا لأبي هاشم الجبائي' highlights the exception.
3
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why this distinction matters. How does it affect our understanding of faith and the role of reasoned reflection?
This consensus shapes later debates about the requirements for valid faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الذين يتفكرون في خلق السَّماوات والأرض ؛ فإنَّهم كلّهم مِن أهل النظر والاستدلال. وحكى الآمدي اتفاق الأصحاب على انتفاء كفر المقلد، وأنه ليس للجمهور إلا القول بعصيانه بترك النظر إن قدر عليه، مع اتفاقهم على صحة إيمانه، وأنه لا يُعرَف القولُ بعدم صحة إيمان المقلد إلا لأبي هاشم الجبائي من المعتزلة.
Regarding those who reflect upon the creation of the heavens and the earth: indeed all of them are from the people of reasoned reflection and inference. And Al-Amidi reported the agreement of the companions (al-ashāb) on the negation of disbelief of the imitator (al-muqallid), and that there is no statement for the majority except that he is sinful for abandoning reflective inquiry if he is capable, along with their agreement on the validity of his faith, and that the statement of the invalidity of the faith of the imitator is not known except from Abu Hashim al-Jubbā'i among the Mu'tazila.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage reports two key points regarding the faith of the muqallid (one who believes without personal reasoned reflection). First, it establishes the context: those who ponder creation are all practitioners of nazar (reasoned reflection) and istidlāl (inference). Then it transmits Al-Amidi's report that the majority of scholars (al-ashāb) agree that the muqallid is not a disbeliever (kāfir). However, if he is capable of nazar and deliberately abandons it, he is sinful (asi). Nevertheless, his faith (īmān) is still valid (saḥiḥ). The only exception is Abu Hashim al-Jubbā'i from the Mu'tazila, who held that the muqallid's faith is not valid. This frames the later discussion about the reality of the muqallid's belief and the nature of the disagreement.

Al-Maturidi on the Common People's Faith
Al-Maturidi states that common people are true believers due to innate rational reflection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Maturidi's position that common people are true believers because their innate disposition provides sufficient rational reflection, even without technical theological language.

Core Idea
Common people are true believers because their fitra gives sufficient rational reflection.

In this passage, Al-Maturidi addresses an important question: Do common people need formal theological training to be considered true believers? He affirms the consensus of his school that they are believers and will fill Paradise, but explains that they possess a sufficient rational reflection through their innate disposition (fitra). This lesson explores that reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words and structure sink in.
Notice the phrase 'حشو الجنة' – the stuffing of Paradise – a traditional expression of affirmation.
2
Identify the consensus claim
The passage begins with a consensus statement: Al-Maturidi's companions agree that common people are true believers who know their Lord. Highlight this as the core thesis.
The consensus is based on transmitted reports and rational reflection.
3
Understand the role of fitra
Al-Maturidi explains that common people have a sufficient amount of rational reflection (nazar 'aqlī) because their innate disposition (fitra) is created upon the oneness of the Maker and the origination of all else.
This fitra-based knowledge does not require technical articulation.
4
Consider the objection and response
Some might argue that rational reflection is necessary. Al-Maturidi responds that the common people already have enough; their inability to express it in theologians' terms is not a deficiency.
Knowledge of expression is extra and not obligatory.
5
Reflect on implications
Think about how this view balances faith and reason. It affirms the validity of common people's faith while maintaining that rational reflection (via fitra) is present.
This prevents elitism in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال أبو منصور الماتريدي : أجمع أصحابُنَا على أنَّ العوام مؤمنونَ عارفونَ بربِّهم، وأنَّهم حَشْو الجنَّة، كما جاءت به الأخبار، وانعقد عليه الإجماع ، لكن منهم مَن قال : لا بُدَّ مِنْ نظر عقلي في العقائد، وقد حصل لهم منه القَدْرُ الكافي، فإنَّ فطرتهم جُبلت على توحيد الصانع وقدمه، وحدوث ما سواه مِن المَوجودات، وإنْ عَجَزُوا عن التّعبير عنه باصطلاح المتكلمين، والعلم بالعِبارة علم زائد لا يلزمهم، والله أعلم.
And Abu Mansur al-Maturidi said: Our companions are in consensus that the common people (al-'awāmm) are believers, knowledgeable of their Lord, and that they are the stuffing of Paradise (ḥashw al-janna), as the reports have come and the consensus has been established upon it. However, among them is one who said: There must be rational reflection (nazar 'aqlī) in the doctrines, and they have obtained from it a sufficient amount. For their innate disposition (fiṭrah) has been created upon the oneness of the Maker and His pre-eternity, and the origination of everything other than Him among existents, even if they are incapable of expressing it in the terminology of the theologians (muṣṭalaḥ al-mutakallimīn). And knowledge of the expression is an extra knowledge that is not incumbent upon them. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents the Maturidi position on the faith of the common people ('awāmm). It begins by affirming the consensus of al-Maturidi's followers that the common people are true believers who have ma'rifa (knowledge) of their Lord. The phrase 'ḥashw al-janna' (stuffing of Paradise) is a traditional expression indicating that they will fill Paradise, based on transmitted reports. Al-Maturidi then addresses an objection: some might argue that rational nazar is necessary for correct belief. He responds by explaining that the common people have already obtained a sufficient amount of such reflection through their innate fitra (innate disposition), which is naturally inclined to affirm the oneness and pre-eternity of the Creator and the origination of all other things. Even if they cannot articulate this in the technical language of theologians (mutakallimūn), that does not harm their faith, because knowledge of the technical expression is an additional knowledge not required of them. The passage concludes by deferring ultimate knowledge to God.

Key Terms
العوام
النظر العقلي
الفطرة
توحيد الصانع
اصطلاح المتكلمين
حشو الجنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Common people have no rational reflection or knowledge of God.
Correction: Al-Maturidi asserts that common people do have rational reflection through their innate disposition (fitra), which provides sufficient knowledge of God, even if they cannot express it theologically.

Checkpoint

A) They are incapable of learning such terminology.
B) Knowledge of the expression is an extra knowledge not required.
C) Their beliefs are different from those of theologians.
D) They have no rational reflection at all.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi affirms that common people are true believers because their innate disposition gives them enough rational reflection, even without theological language.

Taj al-Subki's Clarification: Verbal Disagreement
Taj al-Subki clarifies that the disagreement about the muqallid is verbal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the scholarly disagreement about whether the muqallid (imitator) has valid faith is a verbal disagreement, not a substantive one, when properly understood.

Core Idea
The apparent conflict over the muqallid's faith dissolves when its true nature is uncovered.

This passage from the commentary on the Jawharah cites Taj al-Subki's investigation into the faith of the muqallid. The commentator explains that this investigation reveals what appears to be a substantive disagreement among scholars is actually verbal. By reading this lesson, you will see how careful clarification can reconcile differing positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of investigation
Notice that the passage begins by quoting the matn verse which states 'and some of them have investigated and uncovered it.' The 'it' refers to the condition of the muqallid's faith.
The commentator identifies 'some of them' as Taj al-Subki and similar scholars.
2
Understand 'investigation' and 'uncovering'
The terms 'ḥaqqaqa' (investigated) and 'kashfā' (uncovered) are explained as clarifying the true reality of the muqallid's faith, not simply proving it by evidence.
This is a deeper, revelatory clarification.
3
See how the disagreement becomes verbal
The outcome of this investigation is that the disagreement among scholars becomes 'lafẓī' (verbal). This means that when the true nature is understood, the differing expressions do not reflect a real substantive conflict.
All parties ultimately agree on the validity of the muqallid's faith in this world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[القول بلفظية الخلاف في إيمان المقلد] (وَبَعْضُهُمْ حَقَّقَ فِيهِ الكَشْفَا ) أي : وبعض القوم كالتاج السُّبْكي الكشفَ أي البيانَ عن حال إيمان المقلد، وبيَّن حقيقته على الوجه الحقِّ المطابق للواقع، بما يصير به الخلاف لفظيًّا
[The statement that the disagreement regarding the faith of the imitator is verbal] (And some of them have investigated [ḥaqqaqa] and uncovered it [al-kashfā]) – meaning: Some of the people, such as al-Tāj al-Subkī, uncovered, i.e., clarified, the condition of the faith of the imitator, and explained its true nature upon the correct way conforming to reality, by which the disagreement becomes verbal.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a line from the matn (verse) of the Jawharah. The line reads: 'And some of them have investigated (ḥaqqaqa) and uncovered (al-kashfā) it.' The commentator identifies 'some of them' as scholars like Taj al-Subki. The word 'ḥaqqaqa' here means to clarify or to express something in its true form according to the truth, not just to prove by evidence. 'Al-kashfā' means uncovering or making clear. The scholar's investigation into the muqallid's faith reveals its true nature, such that the apparent disagreement among scholars becomes merely verbal (lafẓī) rather than a real substantive difference. This suggests that when properly understood, all parties ultimately agree on the validity of the muqallid's faith in this world, and the conflict is only in wording.

Key Terms
الخلاف اللفظي
التحقيق
الكشف
التاج السبكي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The phrase 'verbal disagreement' means the issue is unimportant or merely about pronunciation.
Correction: It actually means that differing formulations are reconciled when the true nature is uncovered; the disagreement is not substantive but still involves important theological clarification.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator mean by saying the disagreement becomes 'lafẓī' (verbal)?
A) The scholars are using different languages.
B) The apparent disagreement is not substantive when properly understood.
C) The muqallid's faith is a matter of pronunciation only.
D) The disagreement is about the wording of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The disagreement about the muqallid's faith is verbal, not substantive, when properly understood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas of your own faith where you rely on imitation (taqlid) rather than personal conviction? How does Taj al-Subki's clarification challenge you to seek true understanding?
?
When you encounter differing opinions among scholars, do you rush to judge them as contradictory, or do you seek the underlying reconciliation? What prevents you from doing so?
?
The disagreement about the muqallid is resolved by 'uncovering' the true nature. What unresolved doubts or confusions in your own faith might be resolved if you sincerely investigated the truth of the matter?

The Sufficiency of Fitrah for Faith
Response to the objection that rational reflection is required for faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the common people possess sufficient rational reflection through their innate disposition (fitrah) for valid faith, even without technical theological argumentation.

Core Idea
Fitrah provides the essential knowledge of tawḥīd; technical terms are extra.

This passage addresses a key objection: Is rational reflection (naẓar) necessary for every believer? Some scholars insisted on it, but the author defends ordinary Muslims. He argues that their very nature (fitrah) already embodies the basic truths, even if they cannot articulate them in the jargon of kalām. Let us examine how this fitrah-based argument works.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
Notice that some scholars demand rational proof for doctrinal beliefs. This sets up the tension.
The consensus on verbal dispute is mentioned first.
2
Understand the fitrah counter-argument
The author claims the common people already have the sufficient level of reflection through their God-given natural disposition.
Fitrah is created upon tawḥīd.
3
Appreciate the nuance on terminology
He distinguishes between the essential belief and the technical expression. Knowledge of terms is superadded and not obligatory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول بلفظية الخلاف في إيمان المقلد الإجماع، لكن منهم من قال: لا بد من نظر عقلي في العقائد، وقد حصل لهم منه القدر الكافي، فإن فطرتهم جبلت على توحيد الصانع وقدمه، وحدوث ما سواه من الموجودات، وإن عجزوا عن التعبير عنه باصطلاح المتكلمين، والعلم بالعبارة علم زائد لا يلزمهم، والله أعلم. وبعضهم حقق فيه الكشفا أي: وبعض القوم كالتاج السبكي الكشف أي البيان عن حال إيمان المقلد، وبين حقيقته على الوجه الحق المطابق للواقع، بما يصير به الخلاف لفظيا. فقال: إن يجزم المقلد الذي فيه أهلية النظر، ولا يخشى عليه من الخوض فيه الوقوع في الشبه والضلال، اعتقاده بصدق قول الغير أي الذي أخبره به غير المعصوم دون حجة، وكان جزما مطابقا للواقع من غير شك ولا ترديد على وجه يقع معه في نفسه أنه عالم بما جزم به؛ عالم صح إيمانه، وكفى عند أهل السنة الأشعري وغيره في إجراء الأحكام الدنيوية من الصلاة عليه وغيره.
Statement on the verbal nature of the disagreement concerning the faith of the muqallid. [There is] consensus, but among them are those who said: There must be rational reflection (naẓar 'aqlī) in doctrinal matters, and [the common people] have already obtained from it the sufficient amount, for their natural disposition (fiṭrah) was created upon the oneness (tawḥīd) of the Maker and His pre-eternity, and the temporal origination of everything else, even if they are incapable of expressing it in the terminology of the mutakallimīn. And knowledge of the expression is superadded knowledge that is not obligatory upon them. And Allah knows best. And some of them realized the unveiling (kashf) therein, i.e., some of the people, like Tāj al-Subkī, [made] the unveiling, i.e., the clarification, concerning the state of the faith of the muqallid, and explained its reality in the true way corresponding to reality, by which the disagreement becomes verbal. Then he said: If the muqallid—who has the capacity for reflection (ahlīyat al-naẓar) and is not feared to fall into doubts and misguidance if he engages in it—has certainty (jazzm) in his belief in the truthfulness of the statement of another, i.e., that which someone other than an infallible [prophet] informed him without proof, and [that certainty] is a certainty matching reality, without doubt or hesitation, in such a way that he feels in himself that he is knowledgeable regarding that which he is certain, then his faith is valid and sufficient according to Ahl al-Sunna al-Ash'arī and others for the execution of worldly rulings, such as praying over him, etc.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and resolution of the dispute regarding the faith of the common follower (muqallid) who does not independently derive proofs. The author begins by stating that there is a consensus among scholars, but some hold that rational reflection (naẓar 'aqlī) is necessary for creedal beliefs. However, he counters that the common people already possess sufficient reflection through their natural disposition (fiṭrah), which is created upon tawḥīd and belief in the temporal origination of creation, even if they cannot articulate this in technical theological terms. Knowledge of terminology is an extra knowledge not required of them. Then some scholars, like Tāj al-Subkī, clarified the issue, making it a verbal dispute (khilāf lafḍī) by explaining the reality of the muqallid's faith correctly. Finally, the passage states the condition that if the muqallid, who has the capacity for reflection and is not prone to falling into doubts, attains certainty (jazzm) in the truth of what he was told by another (non-infallible) without proof, and this certainty is firm and conviction-producing, then his faith is valid and sufficient for worldly rulings (like funeral prayers) according to Ahl al-Sunna and al-Ash'arī.

Key Terms
مقلد
فطرة
نظر عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that without formal rational proofs, one's faith is invalid.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the innate fitrah provides sufficient knowledge, even without formal theological reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why do common people have sufficient rational reflection?
A) They studied theology.
B) Their fitrah is created upon tawḥīd.
C) They memorized the Ash'arī creed.
D) They follow a particular scholar.

Key Takeaway
Fitrah provides the essential rational reflection for faith, making technical terminology unnecessary for valid belief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt spiritually inadequate because you couldn't articulate your beliefs as eloquently as scholars? How does this passage free you from that pressure, and what does it mean for your trust in God's guidance through fitrah?
?
Think of someone you know whose faith is simple yet firm. Have you ever subtly looked down on them for lacking theological sophistication? How does this passage challenge you to honour their fitrah-based certainty as sufficient before God?
?
When you learn theological terms, do you seek them to deepen your understanding or to feel superior to others? What would it look like to approach knowledge with the humility this passage teaches—knowing that fitrah already gave you the essentials?

The Condition of Certainty for the Muqallid's Faith
Clarification of the muqallid's faith condition by Tāj al-Subkī.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific condition under which a muqallid's faith is considered valid and how this resolves the dispute into a verbal one.

Core Idea
Certainty (jazzm) in the conveyed truth, not independent proof, suffices for valid faith.

The previous lesson established that fitrah provides basic knowledge. Now the author presents a detailed clarification by Tāj al-Subkī to settle the dispute. He identifies the essential condition: the muqallid must have unwavering certainty (jazzm) in what he follows, even without proof. This resolves the apparent disagreement, showing it is only verbal. Let's explore this condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the clarifying scholar
Tāj al-Subkī is mentioned as one who uncovered the true nature of the muqallid's faith.
2
Understand the preconditions
The muqallid must have the capacity for reflection (ahlīyat al-naẓar) and not be prone to doubts.
This ensures that the person is not simply ignorant but capable of deeper thought.
3
Grasp the core condition: certainty
The muqallid must have firm certainty (jazzm) in the truth of what he was told, matching reality.
The certainty must be such that he feels knowledgeable.
4
Apply the ruling
Such faith upholds worldly duties like funeral prayer according to Ahl al-Sunna.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

According to the passage, what must the muqallid's certainty (jazzm) be?

Key Takeaway
Faith based on sincere certainty (jazzm) is valid even without independent proof, provided the person has the capacity for reflection and is safe from doubt.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you possess the same level of certainty in your faith that Tāj al-Subkī describes for the muqallid, or do you find yourself plagued by doubts and hesitations? What will you do today to strengthen your certainty?
?
This lesson says the muqallid 'feels in himself that he is knowledgeable' about what he is certain of. Do you feel personally knowledgeable about your beliefs, or are you simply following without understanding? What is one specific belief you will strive to understand more deeply this week?
?
The condition 'not feared to fall into doubt' suggests some people are vulnerable. Are you someone who can safely engage in theological reflection, or do you avoid it for fear of losing your faith? What does your answer reveal about the state of your heart, and what concrete step can you take to address it?
